In the endless night of darkness, they remain trapped for ten thousand kalpas, with no escape. Even if they are reborn, they return as beasts or non-human beings, forever losing the human Way.

In summary, the causes and conditions of the scriptural Dharma are thus exhausted. When opening and reciting this sutra, examine it closely.

*Essential Rituals and Precepts: Wondrous Scrolls, Volume Two* concludes.
*Essential Rituals and Precepts: Annotated Excerpts, Volume Three* begins.
Compiled by Daoist Zhu Faman of the Three Caverns.

**Statutes state:** When laypeople receive registers, though they may not yet be familiar with the statutes and methods, they come to inquire about their doubts. Answer truthfully according to your understanding; do not deceive or falsely claim ignorance. You must admonish evil and return to goodness, encourage right and eliminate wrong. Do not form cliques, act for private gain, harm the Dharma, or mislead yourself and others.

**The Statutes of the Nine Punishments state:** High-virtue Daoists, Grand Masters of Rites, and the Eighty-One Worthies—when they purify themselves and ascend the altar, the altar being the Hall of Lofty Emptiness, the Altar of the August Spirits—need not receive the Dharma or registers to transmit registers and rites. Only the Twenty-Four Scrolls, containing the subtle and essential words, the Three Caverns’ treasure scriptures, and all secret talismans and taboo names, must be received from a master. The master must be one who has fully received the teachings as a Daoist disciple, a person of abundant virtue. Even the descendants of the Celestial Master, though they may be masters, must observe the rites of disciples. When the descendants of the Celestial Master receive the various Dharmas, the ritual fees must follow the common people’s standards and may not be omitted; omission will incur no mercy from the Three Offices.

**Statutes state:** Those who receive various contracts and natural contracts, then transmit contracts without possessing the scriptures, are false and deceitful masters. Contracts originate from the scriptures of deliverance; without scriptures, what contract can there be? A master must copy the scriptures and take vows before becoming a master. Those who have not yet reached the required years may not serve as masters; they should seek a fully qualified master for their former disciples. Those who are not fully qualified may temporarily receive disciples, with the goal of becoming fully qualified. There is no limit to the number of masters, nor restrictions on their gender. Masters may not imprison their disciples or prevent them from betraying their original master. Those who betray their master will endure the tenfold torments of suffering and be reborn among the six kinds of beasts. Those who imprison their disciples will be bound in dark prisons and later reborn in remote borderlands. Let the foolish and ignorant take heed.

**Statutes state:** The treasure scriptures are of utmost importance, appearing but once in ten thousand kalpas. As the fortunes of heaven and earth hasten, the worthy are needed to assist the correct. Transmission is permitted once every seven hundred years. If one has the proper karmic affinity, the scriptures may be transmitted after seven years; within three years, there will be no disasters or hardships. After four years, transmission may proceed. If transmitted to the right person, both teacher and disciple share in the blessing; if transmitted to the wrong person, both share the same sin, together enduring the punishments of fire, water, wind, knives, and the labor of carrying stones for ten thousand kalpas.

**Statutes state:** In all matters concerning the master, act as if holding an object with both hands at heart level, taking care not to drop or lose it. The master is like a father. Yet in serving the Dao and attending the master, there are two kinds of people: one is nominal, the other substantive. Those who merely claim affiliation with the master’s gate, without cultivating wondrous principles, great merit, or non-action, encounter obstacles everywhere. They generate afflictions and birth-and-death, unable to know the constant. Their reckless actions lead to misfortune—this is nominal service to the master. But those who wholeheartedly rely on the master, whose every action accords with the Dharma, who establish vast fields of merit, deliver themselves and others, act without acting yet accomplish all, wherever they go enjoy unimpeded benefit, free from all obstacles, attain immortality and deathlessness, and forever enjoy purity and good fortune—this is substantive service to the master.

**Statutes state:** When the master speaks to disciples, they address one another as “I,” “we,” “you,” and “disciples,” for they are as close as family. In all matters concerning the master’s gate, outwardly one is called “disciple,” inwardly one is called by name, not by surname. The master’s master is treated as a patriarch; the patriarch’s master is treated as a great-grandfather; the great-grandfather’s master is treated as a great-great-grandfather; the great-great-grandfather’s master is called the ancestral master. From the ancestral master to the disciple’s own body constitutes five generations. Pay reverence to the patriarch with a single bow and inquire after his well-being. In written correspondence, address all as “disciple,” never using disrespectful language. Violations incur the penalties of the Seven Punishments.

**Statutes state:** Treat those who have preceded you in attainment as you would your master. Those who are capable of being masters are called “masters of the heart.” When paying respects, if all the masters are present, first bow to the ancestral master, then inquire after the well-being of the patriarch, then the register master, and then the preceptor.

**Statutes state:** In all formal correspondence and memorials, disciples must write in standard script, never in cursive. Violations incur the penalties of the Five Punishments. Daoists must always defer to those who have preceded them in attainment, standing one step below their master.

**Precepts state:** When disciples pay respects to their master, they must do so in court dress on the first and fifteenth of each month. They may not speak duplicitously, harboring private thoughts while uttering public words, or speak inconsistently behind their master’s back.

**Precepts state:** When Daoist disciples and their master set an appointment, failing to keep it binds their souls and confines them under the jurisdiction of the Water Office for fifty days of labor.

**Precepts state:** If disciples show disrespect to their master, behaving irreverently and without propriety, addressing their master with familiar pronouns like “I” and “you,” their souls will be detained and confined by the Water Office for sixty days. After the punishment is completed, their lifespan will be reduced by twenty-four years.

**Precepts state:** Disciples must serve their master with the same reverence they show their parents; the master must regard disciples with the same care as for their own children. Violations incur a penalty of twelve years’ reduction in lifespan for each party.

**Precepts state:** In the Orthodox Unity tradition, the three masters are: the first is the proper master, the second is the Celestial Master, and the third is the preceptor. The registers of the Three and Five Offices are equivalent to the three masters and must be equally revered. Violations incur thirty lashes of punishment.

**Precepts state:** When disciples write to their master, they must begin with a formal address, using their full name, and may not behave disrespectfully. Violations incur fifteen strokes of the rod.

**Precepts state:** When Daoist followers speak with their master, they must address themselves as "Daoist follower" and may not use their personal name. Violations incur ten strokes of punishment.

**Precepts state:** If a disciple parts from their master for a month, they must bow three times on the last day of the month, the first day of the new moon, and the three auspicious days of the Three Origins. If the master is not seen on these days, the disciple must still perform the bows. Violations incur ten strokes of punishment for the master; if the disciple is gravely ill for twenty days, the penalty is doubled.

**Precepts state:** Those of the same seven generations may not serve as master and disciple to one another; if they do, their lifespan is reduced by three twelve-year cycles. Yet this precept exists lest a disciple’s transgressions implicate their nine generations of ancestors and extend to the master’s forebears. If a disciple remains loyal and unblemished by the Seven Precepts, their virtue aligning with the Ten Practices and their bone structure matching the celestial charts, even a father may transmit the Dao to his son, enabling all nine generations to ascend as immortals—how then could they incur the penalty of reduced lifespan?

**Precepts state:** If a disciple learns of their master’s illness, distress, poverty, or hardship and fails to respond with a pained heart, they lack benevolence and filial piety, violating their oath. This incurs fifty days of punishment; if the master is gravely ill, one hundred days.

**Precepts state:** Daoists may not, in the presence of another’s disciples, speak ill of or curse that disciple’s master or elders. All discussions must remain impartial; if they speak of good and evil, the soul and spirit within the body will depart. Violations incur fifty days of illness under the scrutiny of the Water Office.

**Precepts state:** If a disciple speaks ill of their master or parents in the presence of others, the celestial officials within their body will report this to the Heavenly Bureau, incurring one hundred days of illness and thirty days of punishment.

**Precepts state:** Upon receiving a letter from their master, disciples must burn incense, hold their tablet, adjust their robes and headdress, bow three times, and then open and read the letter. This is how one reveres the exalted sages, propagates the Dao, and fulfills filial piety—thus aligning with the hearts of the ancient worthies. Violations incur a penalty of one twelve-year cycle in lifespan.

**Precepts state:** If a disciple parts from their master for less than a month, they must wear their headdress, hold their tablet, and greet the master upon return, not appearing in plain clothes. Violations incur a penalty of three *dou* of oil; refusal to comply results in twenty strokes of the rod.

**Precepts state:** If a disciple parts from their master for more than one night but fewer than five, they must adjust their robes, hold their tablet, bow twice, and greet the master according to ritual upon return. Violations incur a penalty of five *liang* of incense, to be offered to the Three Treasures.

Disciples must address their master by name. When visiting their master, they may not sit without permission; they must wait for the master’s command. Violations incur ten days of illness.

**Precepts state:** Disciples may not address their master as "Daoist" but must refer to them as "my master," "harmonious master," "great master," "revered master," or "master." Violations incur a penalty of three *dou* of oil, to be offered to the Three Treasures, and ten days of illness.

Disciples may not violate the master’s name taboos. If a senior figure questions them insistently, they must respond softly with their name. Violations incur a penalty of one twelve-year cycle in lifespan.

Disciples may not act rudely toward those at the master’s side, for attendant spirits observe and scrutinize. Violations incur a penalty of one twelve-year cycle in lifespan.

If a disciple witnesses the master’s faults, they must kneel in a secluded place, hands clasped respectfully, and offer remonstrance. They may not speak of the master’s errors or shameful matters in the presence of others. Violations incur a penalty of one twelve-year cycle in lifespan.

When sharing a room with their master, disciples may not lie down before the master or rise after them. Violations incur fifty days of illness.

When eating with their master, disciples may not begin before the master; they must always follow the master’s lead. If the master commands them to eat first, the disciple may do so but must stop when the master does.

In all matters of daily life, movement, travel, and conduct, disciples must remain humble, reverent, and respectful toward their master, never lapsing in propriety for even a moment. Violations incur thirty strokes of punishment.

If a disciple serves a master younger than themselves, they must still show reverence, treating the master as they would an official of two thousand *shi* or their own parents. They may not rely on their seniority to slight the master. Violations incur eighty strokes of the rod; the master must discipline the disciple accordingly.

When seated with their master, if the master rises, moves, or departs, the disciple must rise and step aside, not remaining seated in composure. Violations incur thirty strokes of punishment.

If a disciple has not yet received the precepts and rituals, or if they are of advanced age or similar disposition, and later seeks the master’s teachings, they must still serve as a disciple, addressing the master by name with reverence, not as before. Violations incur forty strokes of punishment.

If a disciple shares the same surname or clan as the master, or is related as cousin, nephew, or grandnephew, and later seeks the master’s teachings, they must still remain humble and reverent, addressing the master by name, not as an ordinary acquaintance. Violations incur forty strokes of the rod; the master must discipline the disciple accordingly.

Those related to the master within the fifth degree of mourning may not serve as master and disciple. This is because the transmission of the Dao involves witnessing the salvation of three generations of ancestors and seven generations of forebears, binding the disciple’s descendants to the covenant. If the covenant is violated, the nine generations of ancestors will be punished, and the disciple will suffer one hundred days of illness.

Disciples may not harbor anger, resentment, or hatred toward their master, nor hold grudges in their heart. Violations incur thirty strokes of punishment.

When seeking the Dao and its teachings from their master, disciples must wear their headdress, hold their tablet, and humbly entreat, not acting presumptuously. Violations incur forty strokes of punishment.

Disciples may not argue with their master. Violations incur forty strokes of punishment.

When serving their master, if the master returns from a journey, the disciple must greet them along the way, regardless of distance, and may not remain seated in composure awaiting the master’s arrival. Violations incur forty strokes of punishment.

When walking with their master, disciples may not step on the master’s shadow. Violations incur thirty strokes of punishment.

In all matters of coming and going, and in all actions, disciples must first report to and seek the master’s approval; they may not act on their own authority. Violations incur fifty strokes of punishment.

When serving their master, disciples must remain sincere and unwavering, not harboring doubt or divided loyalty. Violations incur thirty strokes of punishment.

Disciples may not steal, reveal, or misuse the master’s utensils, containers, money, belongings, food, or prohibited teachings and precepts.

Violations incur thirty strokes of punishment.

When seated with their master, disciples may not spit or soil the surroundings. Violations incur twenty strokes of punishment.

When attending their master in the presence of guests, disciples must stand guard at the master’s side and may not arrogantly occupy a grand seat as though no different from commoners. Violations incur seventy strokes of punishment.

After receiving the master’s teachings and achieving moral accomplishment, should disciples be revered by others, they must remain humble and ever mindful of the master’s grace, never forgetting their debt. Violations incur a penalty of two twelve-year cycles.

The precepts state: Daoist priests and nuns, though advanced in years, if their attainment in the Dao and precepts is inferior, may not precede those of lesser age but greater mastery in the Way. Those younger in years but superior in precept and discipline may proceed first; this order must not be disrupted. Violations incur sixty strokes of punishment.

The regulations state: When eminent masters reside together, should a cunning disciple seek refuge—whether out of lingering attachment or coercion—they must be instructed with discernment. Some may first be assigned arduous tasks, others enticed with sweet words; some may be left to sit in oblivion, others banished afar; some may be granted scriptural treasures or teachings, others ordered to fast and purify themselves; or, fearing their own authority, the master may delegate others to soothe and receive them. If, despite all efforts, the disciple remains incorrigible and shows no sign of improvement, keeping such a disciple will extinguish the Dao and its teachings; they must be permanently cast out.

The regulations state: Should a disciple delight in the true Dharma, deeply comprehend the profound sutras, and earnestly desire to hear more, yet their capacity is hindered by past karma or dull faculties, such good intentions must be nurtured. They should be comforted with the Three Refuges and Five Precepts, guided toward tranquility, and settled in mind. Eminent Dharma masters, as the refuge of all beings, must adapt to each disposition: those of haughty nature must be humbled; the obstinate must be subdued; the timid must be encouraged toward courage. As parents to all beings, how could they not act with wisdom? Only thus may the disciple align with the Dao and never regress.

The regulations state: Shangqing masters may not administer the Great Precepts, accept disciples as dependents, take on disciples, instruct others, engage in evaluating worldly affairs, travel alone after midnight, or associate with heterodox views. Their every action must accord with the Dharma.

The regulations state: Disciples under one’s care must be reproved all the more sternly. For grave transgressions, they must be rebuked with five admonitions: first, “I now reprove you; you must leave”; second, “After you depart, do not enter my chamber”; third, “Even if I am alone, I shall not employ you in service”;

Fourth, "While I live, do not come near my dwelling"; fifth, "Though I may guide and transform beings of the three wretched paths and five destinies, I shall never speak with you again." Such reproofs and subduing measures are already severe in practice—no further excess is needed. Unlawful punishments such as beating with canes, cangues, or rods, hanging by the neck, binding in reverse, or day-and-night scolding and abuse are not found in the precepts and harm the seed of great compassion.

Should a master command a disciple to buy wine or meat, engage in trade, tend cattle or horses, or commit acts of sexual misconduct, the disciple may say, "Such deeds are not the conduct of the Heavenly Worthy; I dare not comply." If the master grows angry and reproves them, the disciple must depart.

The regulations state: Disciples of evil conduct who show no remorse exhibit ten traits: first, shamefulness in wrongdoing; second, failure to cultivate goodness; third, defiance when admonished; fourth, lack of dignified bearing; fifth, arrogance and pride without reverence; sixth, laziness and cruelty; seventh, association with the unfaithful; eighth, indulgence in licentiousness; ninth, delight in slaughter; tenth, drunkenness that disrupts hierarchy. Those who bear these ten traits destroy the Dharma. If one refrains from evil, endures hardship with humility, enters the chamber at dawn to remove the urinal, and pays respects at morning, noon, and dusk—seeking instruction and obeying commands without shirking labor—then gradually they will fill the great vessel, accumulate virtue, and attain perfection. Such disciples uphold and propagate the true Dharma, and are widely praised.

The regulations state: Male and female officials may each take refuge under their own kind—male officials under male officials, female officials under female officials—with the most accomplished in the Dharma serving as master. Every ten days, they must visit the master to receive instruction. At the appointed time, they shall don their ritual robes, compose their minds with sincerity, and proceed with reverence and solemnity directly to the master’s door, without glancing east or west. Upon arrival, they announce, "This disciple [name] comes to pay respects."

Once the master is prepared, they don the proper attire, burn incense in the sutra-recitation chamber, and summon the disciple to enter. Approaching the master, the disciple performs three measured bows and inquires, "Has the master’s well-being been peaceful of late? Has your virtue remained harmonious?" If the master is without affliction, they reply, "Lately, my recitations have not faltered." If illness or distress is present, the response shall address the matter accordingly.

Should the disciple have committed an offense, the master shall first address it privately, saying, "You have done such-and-such; let my admonition guide your reform." The disciple bows in gratitude and replies, "My lack of discipline has troubled the master’s correction." Bowing again, they vow, "From this day forward, I shall not repeat this transgression." If the meeting occurs before a fast, the fast is observed; afterward, no further arrangements are made, and the disciple departs with two bows, saying, "May the master cherish their well-being."

The master offers final counsel: "Wherever you reside, diligently observe fasts and precepts, guard yourself with care, and never indulge in arrogance." The disciple responds, "Indeed," and retreats three steps before turning away. They may not enter another chamber but must return directly to their own abode and share the master’s admonitions with all, great and small, without concealment.

If a female official lacks the highest Dharma or cannot transmit the teachings, she may seek instruction from a male official. Likewise, female officials of great virtue may invite male officials to receive the Dharma, and the protocols for posture and demeanor apply equally to both male and female officials.

The regulations state: When a master of great virtue returns from afar, they shall send an attendant ahead to the abode to announce their arrival. Disciples must then don their robes and go forth to welcome them, observing proper order among all, without neglecting courtesy or leaving others unprepared.

The regulations state: When a disciple wishes to address the master, a fellow abode-dweller, or a senior from another abode, they must don their robes and approach the chamber. If the door is closed, they shall lightly tap it three times with a bent finger. If no response comes after a brief pause, they tap three more times; if still unanswered, they tap three more times. If silence persists, they must withdraw.

The regulations state: In the disciple’s duties, when the master receives guests, they shall dust the bedding and mats, sweep the area before and behind, and await the master’s command before summoning the guest to enter.

The regulations state: When the master passes an object to a disciple, if the master stands, the disciple stands to receive it; if the master sits, the disciple kneels to receive it.

The regulations state: When visiting or attending the master or a senior of great virtue, the disciple shall bow twice and stand in attendance. If commanded to sit, they bow before doing so. If further matters are discussed, they rise to answer, standing upright with feet together and hands clasped.

The regulations state: When approaching the quarters of a venerable one, if aware they are within the room or storeroom, and no sound is heard, one shall clear the throat to announce their presence. If unacknowledged, they must withdraw; if acknowledged, they shall give their name and state their purpose. If bidden to enter, they enter; if the door is open, they open it; if closed, they leave it closed. Upon entering, they shall gaze directly at the venerable one, without casting sidelong glances.

The regulations state: Those who have left the household life must ever uphold loyalty and rectitude. In all movements and stillness, the Five Rectifications come first: first, rectify one’s own person; second, rectify one’s disciples and followers; third, rectify the noble and base within the abbey; fourth, rectify the dharma for distant assemblies; fifth, rectify the benighted among the laity.

One who can rectify their own person becomes a model for others and can then rectify their disciples, who will receive instruction with proper decorum. One who can rectify the abbey ensures that all, great and small, follow in harmony. One who can rectify the distant assemblies causes the dharma and its meaning to be proclaimed. One who can rectify the benighted laity widely increases wisdom and understanding. Those who can do thus are called Daoists.

If one’s person is not rectified, they become an object of aversion. If disciples are left untaught, they will despise their master and the abbey’s superiors. Without restraint or distinction, noble and base become confused, and trust and righteousness are forsaken. Distant assemblies will all come to loathe them. Deceiving and oppressing the ignorant, they increase the tally of transgressions. Unable to establish authority or grace, they instead entice the disciples, servants, or children of others—either to seize their possessions for themselves or to engage in clandestine dealings—failing to stand by the upright Way, ever conducting themselves with perversity.

Those of such character and reputation are fugitives from discipline and malefactors to be guarded against. If they cannot be corrected, the king’s law shall apply.

*Essential Rituals and Precepts of the Wondrous Scrolls, Fascicle Three, Concluded*
*Essential Rituals and Precepts, Notes on the Precepts, Fascicle Four*

Zhu Faman of the Three Caverns, a Daoist, compiled these one thousand one hundred precepts and vows in unison. The scriptures serve to restrain evil, while the precepts serve to prevent wrongdoing. Yet their branches and streams are so numerous that they are difficult to apply. Extracting their essentials makes them easier to seek.

*The Esoteric Instructions on Ascending to Perfection* states: "Precepts are the ford that blocks the path of filth and defilement, the barrier that prevents the comings and goings of perverse winds."

*The Regulations of the Thousand Perfected* says: "Those who leave the household life do not associate with the secular world, do not seek fame or profit, do not perform contrived meritorious deeds, but enter stillness and meditative concentration, subdue external demons, and are called ‘pure precepts’—the true and real Dharma Mother."

*The Great Precepts of Contemplating the Body* states: "To have scriptures without precepts is like wishing to cross the sea without a boat or oars, or to have a mouth without a tongue—how can one ferry across the countless beings?"

Scholars who do not seek the scriptures and precepts have not awakened to the root of the true essentials. To remain in a long dream without awakening is truly lamentable.

*The Regulations of the Thousand Perfected* says: "Those who leave the household life and uphold the Three Precepts, Five Precepts, Nine Precepts, Ten Precepts, or even the Three Hundred Great Precepts—within each single precept, there are twenty-four benevolent spirits who protect them, and heavenly beings who make offerings. They do not transgress the multitude of evils. Those who do not believe in the precepts transgress evils all the more, their sins binding them in the hidden darkness, where they receive suffering life after life, falling into blind paths. Whether one violates or upholds the precepts, the blessings gained or sins incurred are the same for both Daoists and laypeople—this principle is impartial, and its merit is equal."

*The Commentary on the Book of Changes* states: "Purifying the mind is called *zhai* (fasting); preventing harm is called *jie* (precepts)."

*The Origin of the Fundamental* says: "What the sages and perfected cultivate is necessarily called *teaching*. What ordinary people practice is called *habitual karma*. Teaching and karma are one and not two."

The so-called One Precept is the arising of the unsurpassed, natural Dao-mind. This is the right cause and the most fundamental root.

There are also Two Precepts, namely the Two Contemplations: *qi*-contemplation and spirit-contemplation, which are concentration and wisdom.

There are also Three Precepts, namely the Three Relinquishments: relinquishing body, life, and wealth.

There are also Four Precepts, namely the Four Entrustments of the Mind: kindness, love, goodness, and forbearance.

There are also Five Precepts, namely the Five Remembrances: contemplating the Dao, one attains purity; contemplating the Dao, one leaves all sensual desires; contemplating the Dao, one’s actions oppose the secular; contemplating the Dao, one has no prohibitions; contemplating the Dao, one transcends form and sound.

*Ascending to the Mystery* also speaks of Five Precepts: If one sees beautiful forms, glory, or splendid colors, one covers the eyes with precepts. If one hears good or bad words, or the five tones, one blocks the ears with precepts. If one encounters the eight delicacies or sweet and fragrant foods, one seals the mouth with precepts. If one covets wealth, the seven treasures, or rare jewels, indulging in extreme desires, one subdues the mind with precepts. If one recalls lewdness or pursues evil deeds, one breaks the feet with precepts. Those who can practice these five things will cause their seven generations of ancestors to be reborn in heaven.

There are also Six Precepts, namely the Six Perfections: diligence, faith, gentleness, perseverance, discernment of disputes, and abstention from wine and lust.

There are also Seven Precepts, namely the Seven Awakenings: knowing the One, knowing the Three, knowing misfortune, knowing fortune, knowing the secular, knowing the Dao, and knowing being and non-being.

There are also Eight Precepts, namely the Eight Abilities: to accept what is not one’s own without asserting oneself; to yield virtue to others; to not desire what others desire; to endure unreasonable demands without contention; to distance oneself from the unprincipled and hide in secluded places; to not reveal one’s mind; to not betray the scriptures and precepts; and to not boast with the mouth, closing off the six senses.

The Six Perfections give rise to the Six Penetrations and the Thirty-Eight Precepts, also called the Six Awakenings or the Six Wisdoms.

Closing off the six senses involves the following: The first precept is that the eyes do not wander, becoming distracted by various floral colors, which leads to the loss of essence and the dissipation of youthful vitality, causing the light to dim and not shine clearly. The second precept is that the ears do not listen indiscriminately, becoming confused by the five tones, which harms the spirit and corrupts integrity, allowing evil sounds and wailing to enter. The third precept is that the nose does not seek excessive fragrances, mixing with foul and rank odors, which easily leads to turbidity, causing the form to lose its clarity and uprightness. The fourth precept is that the mouth does not crave flavors, such as rich and greasy foods, which pollute the five spirits and cause the organs and viscera to become congested and burst. The fifth precept is that the hands do not commit evil, do not steal others’ belongings, or covet profit for oneself, lest calamity arise unawares. The sixth precept is that the mind does not indulge in desires, which disturb the five spirits, harm essence, and scatter *qi*, causing the body to become lost and confused. These six precepts are the great afflictions of the body.

The precepts born of the Six Perfections are as follows: The first is that when one sees someone in dire straits, one saves them from death, and when one sees someone of worldly prominence, one can restrain their perversity, leading to long life and boundless worldly blessings. The second is that when one sees someone impoverished, cold, hungry, or in distress, one sacrifices one’s own resources to bestow alms, making them wealthy and noble, with a myriad-fold karmic reward and joy life after life. The third is that when blood-bearing creatures seek refuge in distress, one can rescue and save them from deadly peril, leading to robust health in this life and avoidance of untimely misfortune. The fourth is that when one bestows kindness upon birds and beasts, or any living beings, cutting from one’s own mouth to feed them without惜, one will be sated life after life and always dwell in blessed lands. The fifth is that when one saves all creatures, including the smallest, enabling them all to thrive without premature death or harm, one will prosper in this life and avoid all misfortunes. The sixth is that when one constantly practices a mind of compassion, pitying and saving all beings, releasing life and delivering from death, the merit is profound, enabling one in this life to turn danger into safety, illness into health, poverty into wealth, and all aspirations to be fulfilled. These six precepts all arise from the mind’s penetration of wisdom, enabling one to bestow virtue and act in accordance with nature.

The Six Penetrations are as follows: Heaven is all-seeing, thus heavenly clarity and wisdom are lofty, with wisdom penetrating without end. From above and below, the four directions and eight quarters, nothing is not illuminated, nothing is not understood. Though called a single penetration of wisdom, it encompasses the five colors. Heaven’s penetration is natural, thus it is without harm.

Just as humans emulate Heaven, the crimson pupil of the eye likewise possesses the radiance of the five colors. If one does not uphold the precepts, the qi of the six senses will not flow through the human apertures, and wisdom will remain unopened. Gazing widely upon the five colors causes the eye’s spirit to scatter, its luminous essence to dim and obscure, leading to blindness. Thus this precept is established to deliver heavenly and earthly beings, enabling all men and women to hear the Dharma’s sound. Should one wish to transcend suffering and attain long life, one must uphold this precept, practice it diligently without forgetfulness, guard against wanton gazing, restrain the spirit of the eyes, and cherish and nurture the eye’s pupil. By contemplating the true and stilling the mind, one enables the penetration of wisdom, perceiving emptiness with clarity. Upholding the precepts to restrain oneself causes the five colors to return to the body; when the five colors naturally return, there is no harm or injury, and one’s years and life may be preserved without end.

The Heavenly Worthy said: Earth embodies penetrating hearing, its wisdom far-reaching and its wisdom all-hearing. Thus its resonance responds to Heaven above and pervades the four directions below, with nothing beyond its reach and nothing unheard. Though called a single penetration of wisdom, it encompasses the five tones. Earth’s penetration is natural, giving rise to the five tones, stable and unmoving, thus free from harm.

Just as humans emulate Earth, the ear contains the spirits of the five sounds. If one does not uphold the precepts, wisdom remains unopened. One must not recklessly listen to the sounds of the five tones, for they cause the five spirits to scatter, the ears to become hollow and desolate, filled with mournful roaring, the five spirits to weep, leading to deafness. Thus this precept is expounded to deliver heavenly and earthly beings, enabling all men and women to universally hear the Dharma’s sound. Should one desire longevity, one must uphold the great precepts to restrain the ear’s spirit, avoiding excessive listening to unwholesome sounds. By quietly listening to the wondrous sutras and the chapters of divine perfected ones, one enables the penetration of wisdom, resonating through the vast emptiness. Upholding the precepts to restrain oneself causes the five tones to return to the body; when the five tones naturally return, the ears are free from evil sounds, and one’s years and life may be preserved for long ages.

The Heavenly Worthy said: The East embodies the penetrating emptiness of primordial yang qi, qi emerging from emptiness, its wisdom bright and its wisdom vast. Thus it is called penetrating emptiness, boundlessly open and without obstruction, the primordial blue qi impossible to exhaust. Though called a single penetration of wisdom, it encompasses the five qi. Emptiness admits nothing, thus it is free from harm.

Though humans possess form, their form likewise emulates emptiness. The nose contains the spirits of yellow tranquility and repelling impurity. If one does not uphold the precepts, wisdom remains unopened. Without entering wisdom, one must not partake of foul and rank substances. The qi of foul and rank things causes the nose’s spirit to become congested, its pathways obstructed, easily severed, its bright beam blocked. Thus this precept is expounded to deliver heavenly and earthly beings, enabling all men and women to hear the Dharma’s sound. Should one wish to transcend the eight difficulties and myriad sufferings, one must receive this precept to restrain the nose’s spirit, avoiding excessive intake of foulness. By cleansing and dredging the bright beam, guiding and drawing forth the qi of life, one enables the penetration of wisdom, the qi of emptiness to enter. Upholding the precepts to restrain oneself causes all qi to return to the body; when all qi naturally return, merging with the qi, one’s body and life are preserved for long ages, never to be destroyed.

The Heavenly Worthy said: The West embodies the penetrating emptiness of lesser yin qi, qi emerging from emptiness, its wisdom subtle and its wisdom clear. Thus it is called penetrating emptiness, boundlessly open and difficult to exhaust, the lesser yin qi. Though called a single penetration of wisdom, it encompasses the five flavors. Emptiness admits nothing and contains nothing. Though humans emulate emptiness, if one does not uphold the precepts, wisdom remains unopened. Without wisdom, the five flavors become afflictions: gluttonous craving for rich and greasy foods causes the five spirits to become unsettled, leading to peril and danger, shortening one’s years and life. Thus this precept is expounded to deliver heavenly and earthly beings, enabling all men and women to universally hear the Dharma’s sound. Should one desire long life, one must uphold the great precepts to restrain the five spirits, guarding against greed for the five flavors. By moderating rich and greasy foods, cleansing and dredging the five viscera, keeping them cool and clear, and cultivating wisdom, the five flavors become harmonious and fragrant, the inner self tranquil and at peace, and one’s years and life remain unharmed.

The Heavenly Worthy said: The South embodies penetrating clarity, the qi of greater yang emerging from clarity, its wisdom clear and its wisdom opened, interlinked and penetratingly clear. Thus the South possesses the Crimson Emperor’s Hall of Penetrating Yang, where the myriad things are smelted and refined, receiving refinement to become luminous. If one does not uphold the precepts, wisdom remains unopened; without wisdom, the heart’s spirit becomes agitated, giving rise to desire and love, causing one to become lost and confused, harming the spirit and injuring life, preventing longevity. Thus this precept is expounded to deliver heavenly and earthly beings, enabling all men and women to universally hear the Dharma’s sound. Should one desire long life and transcend all afflictions, one must receive this precept to restrain the heart’s spirit, eliminating emotions and dispelling desires, abandoning the dust to enter clarity. By enabling the penetration of wisdom and opening the six senses’ pathways, the body enters penetrating clarity, merging with the qi of nature, preserving one’s years and life to attain eternal longevity.

The Heavenly Worthy said: The North is called penetrating subtlety, subtlety born of the mysterious, its wisdom far-reaching and its wisdom profound, subtly penetrating the true. It darkly observes transgressions and blessings, presiding over the origin of life, its hands emulating the four seasons, corresponding above to Heaven’s Gate. As Heaven’s Gate eternally turns, so too do the hands move. The hands possess a great spirit that receives life and fills the directions, governing the four great elements of humans, observing human success and failure. If one does not uphold the precepts, wisdom remains unopened, causing the heart to become agitated, all evils to arise, the hands to act unconsciously, greedily stealing and selfishly scheming, leading to the loss of wisdom and life, failing to complete one’s natural lifespan. Thus this precept is expounded to deliver heavenly and earthly beings. Should one desire long life and transcend evil roots, one must uphold this precept to restrain the hand’s spirit, enabling the penetration of wisdom and the heart’s natural clarity. By preventing the hands from coveting and stealing, evil roots do not arise. Corresponding to Heaven and following the qi, the body enters nature, preserving one’s years and life to attain eternal longevity.

The *Scripture of Ascending Mystery* states: The Nine Precepts of Great Clarity are as follows. The First Precept: The body must not be defiled or impure, indulging in desires and sensual realms, obscuring and inviting bodily misfortune, wantonly self-indulgent, delighting in evil and committing wrongs, adorning oneself with ornate and extravagant finery, seeking comfort in soft and smooth things, wallowing in self-gratification without satiety, unaware of falling into the net of transgressions, unable to awaken to one’s own faults. The body’s transgressions are vast and immeasurable. One must not personally violate this precept nor instruct others to do so. By gathering the mind and upholding the precepts, one practices them throughout life.

This is the First Precept of the Supreme Venerable Supreme Unity.

The Second Precept states: The mind must not harbor evil thoughts or malicious intentions. It must not form judgments or evaluations, covet and strive for gain, obscure and obstruct wealth and profit, indulge in greed and hidden malice, scheme unlawfully, engage in licentiousness and perversion, hold biased and unequal views, harbor jealousy and ignorance, act with self-righteousness and cruelty, or wallow in turbid desires and gluttony without satiety. Unaware of falling into the net of transgressions, unable to awaken to one’s own faults, the sins of the mind are vast and boundless. One must not personally violate this precept nor instruct others to do so. By gathering the mind and upholding the precepts, one practices them throughout life.

This is the Second Precept of the Supreme Venerable Supreme Unity.

The Third Precept states: The mouth must not recklessly speak of good or evil, utter curses or insults, deceive with falsehoods, sow discord with double-tongued words, engage in litigation or accusations, gossip about others’ faults, create disputes or fabricate harm, stir up baseless conflicts, disrupt public order, or indulge in singing, chanting, whistling, or sighing. It must not crave fine flavors or take pleasure in tastes without satiety. Unaware of falling into the net of transgressions, unable to awaken to one’s own faults, the sins of the mouth are vast and boundless. One must not personally violate this precept nor instruct others to do so. By gathering the mind and upholding the precepts, one practices them throughout life.

This is the Third Precept of the Supreme Venerable Supreme Unity.

The Fourth Precept states: The hands must not kill or harm living beings, including crawling, creeping, or blood-bearing creatures. They must not plunder, rob, steal, or take what is not rightfully theirs, nor pick up lost items or accept improper offerings. They must not wield weapons or act unlawfully. Unaware of falling into the net of transgressions, unable to awaken to one’s own faults, the sins of the hands are the gravest of all. One must not personally violate this precept nor instruct others to do so. By gathering the mind and upholding the precepts, one practices them throughout life.

This is the Fourth Precept of the Supreme Venerable Supreme Unity.

The Fifth Precept states: The eyes must not gaze upon what is contrary to the Dao, the law, or righteousness—ornate adornments, seductive appearances, or lustful sights. They must not be dazzled by excess, overflowing with greed, or captivated by finery, jewels, licentiousness, or inauspicious omens. The eyes, as the rear guard, gather all misfortunes, bringing forth calamities and poisons. Sins and transgressions accumulate, all originating from the eyes. The mind, eyes, mouth, and hands are the sources of misfortune, the instigators of disaster, and the repositories of sin. They must not be indulged, nor left unrestrained, nor treated with carelessness. Unaware of falling into the net of transgressions, unable to awaken to one’s own faults, the sins of the eyes are the gravest of all. One must proclaim the precepts for the eyes, exhorting all to observe them. By upholding the eye precepts, thoughts subside; when thoughts cease, the mind becomes empty; when the mind is empty and unattached, one enters the realm of nothingness and attains the wisdom of the Dao. By gathering the mind and upholding the precepts, one practices them without fail throughout life.

This is the Fifth Precept of the Supreme Venerable Supreme Unity.

The Sixth Precept states: The ears must not listen to the eight sounds or five musics, licentious or inauspicious melodies that disrupt righteousness and lead to the downfall of nations, or deceptive and corrupting music without satiety. Unaware of falling into the net of transgressions, unable to awaken to one’s own faults, the sins of the ears are also grave. One must not personally violate this precept nor instruct others to do so. By gathering the mind and upholding the precepts, one practices them throughout life.

This is the Sixth Precept of the Supreme Venerable Supreme Unity.

The Seventh Precept states: The nose must not covet fragrances or despise foul odors, nor recklessly discern good and evil. Unaware of falling into the net of transgressions, unable to awaken to one’s own faults, the sins of the nose are also considerable. One must not personally violate this precept nor instruct others to do so. By gathering the mind and upholding the precepts, one practices them throughout life.

This is the Seventh Precept of the Supreme Venerable Supreme Unity.

The Eighth Precept states: The feet must not tread upon unrighteousness, transgress the law, or traverse evil or inauspicious paths. Unaware of falling into the net of transgressions, unable to awaken to one’s own faults, the sins of the feet are also considerable. One must not personally violate this precept nor instruct others to do so. By gathering the mind and upholding the precepts, one practices them throughout life.

This is the Eighth Precept of the Supreme Venerable Supreme Unity.

The Ninth Precept states: The body must not indulge in reckless abandon, forcibly invoke spirits, or attack what is not its adversary, lest essence dissipate, spirit scatter, and the three qi flee. By giving free rein to desires without satiety, unaware of falling into the net of transgressions, one risks the ruin of one’s clan and lineage, unable to awaken to one’s own faults. Such sins are the gravest of all. One must not personally violate this precept nor instruct others to do so. By gathering the mind and upholding the precepts, one practices them throughout life.

This is the Ninth Precept of the Supreme Venerable Supreme Unity.

There are ten precepts in total. The *Scripture of Resolute Mind* states: To transmit the Dharma of the Ten Precepts, the Dharma master sits facing south while the disciple bows three times toward the master. After paying respects, the disciple requests: "I wish to receive the accomplished Ten Precepts and vow to uphold them throughout my life, swearing never to transgress them." Thereupon, kneeling reverently with focused mind, the disciple visualizes the great sages of the ten directions above and receives the precepts with hands clasped in sincerity. The Dharma master burns incense and expounds the precepts as follows:

First: Do not kill; cherish all living beings.
Second: Do not engage in licentiousness or harbor wayward thoughts.
Third: Do not steal or take unrighteous wealth.

Fourth: Do not deceive; reverse falsehood with truth.
Fifth: Do not become intoxicated; constantly reflect on pure conduct.
Sixth: Maintain harmony among clan and kin, without partiality.
Seventh: Upon witnessing another’s good deeds, rejoice and lend support in mind.
Eighth: Upon seeing another in distress, assist in performing meritorious acts.
Ninth: When others act against me, resolve not to retaliate.
Tenth: Toward all who have yet to attain the Dao, I shall hold no expectations.

These are the Ten Precepts.
The recipient responds: "Disciple [Name] receives them and vows to uphold and cultivate them throughout life."
Then, all rites of reverence to the master are completed.

*Essential Rituals of Compassionate Discipline: Wondrous Scroll, Fascicle Four* concludes.
*Essential Rituals of Compassionate Discipline: Annotated Scroll, Fascicle Five*
Compiled by Daoist Zhu Faman of the Three Caverns

The Heavenly Worthy states: Those who receive these Ten Precepts and cultivate them according to the Dharma shall be guarded and protected by the celestial officials of the ten directions, and shall unfailingly attain the Dao. If worldly entanglements press upon you and you cannot fulfill all ten, then nine, eight, seven, or even five—choose those you are able to uphold. Yet even the merit of the Five Precepts is beyond measure.

The *Scripture of the Great Precepts* states: When performing purification rites or seeking the Dao, one should wholeheartedly request and receive the Ten Precepts, earnestly upholding them without forgetfulness. Focus the mind in silent contemplation, penetrating the natural with unwavering thought. Do not allow scattered thoughts to disturb body and spirit. Those who can do so should listen attentively:

First Precept: Let the mind harbor no malice or jealousy. Do not give rise to hidden harm. Guard the mouth and be cautious of transgressions. Let thoughts dwell upon the Dharma.
Second Precept: Uphold benevolence and refrain from killing. Show compassion and aid all beings. Extend boundless kindness and salvation, bestowing blessings upon all.
Third Precept: Uphold chastity and yield to righteousness. Neither engage in licentiousness nor steal. Constantly cultivate good thoughts, diminishing the self to aid others.
Fourth Precept: Be free from lust and desire. Let the mind not wander. Maintain purity and vigilance, ensuring conduct remains unstained.
Fifth Precept: Let the mouth utter no evil words. Speech should not be ornate or deceitful. Be loyal and upright in both inner and outer conduct, avoiding transgressions of speech.
Sixth Precept: Abstain from alcohol and regulate conduct. Harmonize qi and temperament. Do not harm the spirit or commit manifold evils.
Seventh Precept: Do not envy those who surpass you. Avoid contention over merit and fame. In all matters, yield and withdraw, placing others before oneself.
Eighth Precept: Do not criticize or disparage the scriptures and teachings. Receive the Dharma with reverence, ever as though in the presence of the divine.
Ninth Precept: Do not engage in quarrels or slander. Do not judge or disparage the four classes of beings. Heaven and humanity will condemn such actions, which harm the spirit and qi.
Tenth Precept: In all actions and conduct, maintain equanimity and single-mindedness. Let harmony prevail among people and tranquility among spirits. Let this be the constant practice.

The Ten Meritorious Exhortations:
First: Exhort and assist in revering the Three Treasures, making offerings to Dharma masters. This ensures rebirth as a noble, virtuous, and filial person of great talent, honored and esteemed, revered in life, with a flourishing lineage.
Second: Exhort and assist in copying scriptures. This ensures rebirth with keen intelligence, vast learning, and profound understanding, ever encountering sagely eras and the teachings of all scriptures, able to recite their verses.
Third: Exhort and assist in establishing purification rites. This ensures rebirth with an illustrious and fragrant reputation, radiant and pure, with striking features and noble bearing.
Fifth: Exhort and assist in providing Dharma robes for masters. This ensures rebirth with enduring elegance, dwelling in the Central Kingdom, never falling to the borderlands or among barbarians, with upright sons and daughters, adorned in caps and jade pendants.
Sixth: Exhort and assist the sovereign, parents, and the people in loyalty and filial piety. This ensures rebirth with many descendants, virtuous sons and daughters, free from all suffering.
Seventh: Exhort and assist in purificatory rites and scripture recitation. This ensures rebirth without falling into hell, ascending instead to the heavens, paying homage to the sages, swiftly regaining form, and transforming within a royal household. Desires are fulfilled, adornments and attire are abundant, the seven ancestors rejoice together, all good affinities converge, and from beginning to end, one enjoys honor and bliss. The Dharma Wheel turns, and all tread the immortal path.
Eighth: Exhort and assist Daoists and others in scriptural study. This ensures rebirth with penetrating wisdom, impeccable conduct in movement and stillness, ever serving as a teacher to others.
Ninth: Exhort and assist in all acts of almsgiving and admonition. This ensures rebirth with longevity, wealth, and joy, ever free from resentment and sorrow.
Tenth: Exhort and assist all people in relinquishing jealousy and desire, practicing all virtues. This ensures rebirth in peace and happiness, free from calamity and disorder. The sick recover, officials are promoted, and one is revered by all. All is auspicious and blessed. The household is pure and noble, cherished by heaven and humanity, respected by spirits and demons, and ever born in blessed lands.

The *Questions and Answers* states: The Heavenly Worthy’s Ten Precepts, bestowed upon the Immortal Lords:
First: Do not envy or suppress those who surpass you, nor obstruct the worthy and wise.
Second: Do not drink alcohol or indulge in dissipation, defiling the Three Palaces.
Third: Do not engage in licentiousness or perversion, nor covet deceit and cunning.
Fourth: Do not abandon or despise the elderly, sick, poor, or lowly.
Fifth: Do not slander the virtuous or attack fellow disciples.
Sixth: Do not hoard treasures and refuse to bestow alms.
Seventh: Do not kill living beings to sacrifice to the Six Heavenly Ghostly Spirits.
Eighth: Do not discuss scriptures and teachings as though they were empty and absurd.
Ninth: Do not betray the kindness of masters or deceive new disciples.
Tenth: Maintain equanimity and single-mindedness, practicing benevolence and filial piety toward all.

Those who uphold these Ten Precepts, clearly establishing their vows, shall unfailingly receive their due reward.

There are also Ten Prohibitions against Evil, which must not be transgressed:
First: Drinking alcohol and indulging in lust, insatiable in desire.
Second: Secretly harming the world and slandering Daoists.
Third: Disrespecting masters, slighting the Dharma, and arrogantly neglecting the Three Treasures.
Fourth: Stealing scriptures and recklessly proclaiming the essentials of the Dao.
Fifth: Borrowing and not returning, deceiving and misleading the people.
Sixth: Killing living beings for taste, speaking falsely while harboring deceit.
Seventh: Being unfilial, betraying kindness, violating righteousness, and transgressing all prohibitions.
Eighth: Reciting scriptures carelessly, belching and yawning, acting self-righteously.

Ninth: Demanding compliance with one’s will and harboring resentment toward the four assemblies.
Tenth: Distorting the meaning of scriptures, altering or omitting sacred texts, disbelieving in past lives, indulging in personal desires, defiling and disdaining the Four Great Elements, and neglecting the Way of life.
These are the Ten Prohibitions against Evil.
Those who violate them may, in this very life, be wrongfully killed by spirits, examined and punished by officials, live in peril despite apparent safety, encounter misfortune despite virtuous conduct, and find all affairs thwarted.

The Twelve Principles to Follow, as stated in the *Sutra of Great Precepts*:
To achieve deliverance, one must practice the Twelve Principles to Follow.
First: Upon encountering the emergence of true scriptures and the Dharma that liberates all, one should awaken the mind of Daoist aspiration, vowing to ascend to the rank of great sage in future lives.
Second: Constantly cultivate a mind of compassion, wishing for all beings to perceive the Dharma, attain liberation far and wide, and encounter no obstructions.
Third: Delight in the teachings of scriptures, explore their depths, and attain understanding, with a resolute and luminous will to enlighten the ignorant.
Fourth: Follow the instructions of masters, broadly propagate and exhort others, guiding them into the enclosure of the Dharma and far from blind paths.
Fifth: Believe in and turn toward the profound and wondrous, reverently uphold the essentials of scriptures, reciting and studying them morning and evening without laziness or weariness.
Sixth: Do not pursue glory and splendor, sever worldly attachments, focus the mind and steadfastly direct all endeavors toward the Dharma.
Seventh: Diligently recite the great scriptures, wishing for all beings, broadly establish bridges and connections, and create karmic conditions for future lives.
Eighth: Constantly generate a mind of goodness, neither devious nor false, free from jealousy, harm, malice, or envy.
Ninth: Wherever one is reborn, may it always be in a sagely age, continuously encountering the teachings of the *Lingbao* scriptures.
Tenth: Purify the body and uphold the precepts, practice fasting and establish merit, broadly saving all beings, that they may all attain deliverance.
Eleventh: Extensively study and comprehend the teachings, propagate and communicate the shared Dharma, and prepare to join the ranks of gods and humans, universally guiding all to liberation.
Twelfth: Always encounter enlightened masters life after life, receive their teachings, propagate and transform others, deliver countless beings, and with all good intentions, attain the true Dao.

The Fourteen Methods for Governing the Body, as stated in the *Questions of the Superior*:
When speaking to a ruler, one should bring benevolence to the state; to a father, kindness to his children; to a teacher, love for the multitude; to an elder brother, fraternal respect in conduct; to a minister, loyalty to the sovereign; to a child, filial piety toward parents; to a friend, trustworthiness in dealings; to a wife, chastity toward her husband; to a husband, harmony within the household; to a disciple, reverence in ritual; to a rustic, diligence in farming; to a worthy person or Daoist, adherence to the Dao; to a foreigner, each guarding their own domain; to a servant or maid, prudence in service.

The Thirty-Six Precepts, as stated in the *Laojun’s Prohibitions and Precepts*:
Practice non-action, practice gentleness and weakness, practice holding to the feminine—do not act first. These are the three supreme practices of the highest rank.
Practice namelessness, practice purity and tranquility, practice all goodness. These are the three supreme practices of the middle rank.
Practice loyalty and filial piety, practice contentment, practice yielding. These are the three supreme practices of the lowest rank.
These nine practices form the first set, comprising eighty-one chapters, gathered as the abode of the Dao, where the exalted and humble share the same standards.
Those who fully embody the highest rank become immortals; those who practice six attain doubled longevity; those who practice three gain extended years and avoid untimely death.

Precepts against anger and delight, for anger is akin to evil.
Precepts against wasting essence and qi.
Precepts against consuming beings with blood, delighting in their savor.
Precepts against coveting treasures and goods.
Precepts against harming the qi of rulers.
Precepts against violating the Dao.
Precepts against being moved by external things.
Precepts against branching out in the forms and names of the Dao.
Precepts against killing.
Precepts against seeking fame for merit.
These are the nine precepts of the highest rank.

Precepts against being misled by the ears, eyes, nose, or mouth.
Precepts against greed for high rank and forced seeking.
Precepts to always remain humble and yielding.
Precepts against studying heterodox texts.
Precepts against engaging in myriad affairs; one should be discerning in mind and avoid frivolity.
Precepts against seeking fame and reputation.
Precepts against impatience and restlessness.
Precepts against excess and overflow.
Precepts against indulging the body with fine clothes and delicious food.
These are the nine precepts of the middle rank.

Precepts against committing all evils.
Precepts against excessive taboos and prohibitions.
Precepts against praying to and sacrificing to ghosts and spirits.
Precepts against despising poverty and lowliness while forcibly seeking wealth and nobility.
Precepts against violence and aggression.
Precepts against self-righteousness.
Precepts against contending with others over right and wrong; if contention arises, withdraw first.
Precepts against boasting of sagely names and greatness.
Precepts against delighting in warfare.
These are the nine precepts of the lowest rank.
These twenty-seven precepts form the second set, collectively constituting the profound source of the Dao, where the exalted and humble share the same practices.
Those who fully embody the highest rank become immortals; those who uphold eighteen attain doubled longevity; those who uphold nine gain extended years and avoid untimely death.
Together, they form the Thirty-Six Precepts.

There are also Twelve Prohibitions:
Prohibition against immoderate lewdness and play.
Immoderate lewdness and play cause the spirit and qi to lose focus, harming the hun and po souls. When the hun and po are harmed, one is prone to wild and reckless behavior.
Prohibition against excessive laughter and loud shouting.
Excessive laughter and loud shouting cause the spirit and qi to scatter, harming the qi of the intestines. When the intestinal qi is harmed, one is prone to fright, palpitations, trembling, and fear.
Prohibition against sorrow, worry, resentment, and anger.
Sorrow, worry, resentment, and anger cause the hun and po souls to fail in their movements, harming the five viscera. When the five viscera are harmed, the will and mind become perverse, leading to delusional speech.
Prohibition against extreme drunkenness and overeating.
Extreme drunkenness and overeating cause the spirit and qi to fail in their containment, harming the six bowels. When the six bowels are harmed, abscesses and ulcers arise without reason.
Prohibition against prolonged grief and prolonged thought.
Prolonged grief and thought cause the spirit and qi to lose their regularity, harming the yin qi. When the yin qi is harmed, one is prone to self-terror and anxiety.
Prohibition against prolonged speech and conversation.
Prolonged speech and conversation cause the spirit and qi to dissipate, harming the yang qi. When the yang qi is harmed, one becomes absent-minded and prone to forgetfulness.
Prohibition against prolonged standing.
Prolonged standing causes the spirit and qi to be exhausted and severed, harming the bone marrow. When the bone marrow is harmed, the joints become stiff and fluid flows improperly.
Prohibition against prolonged sitting.
Prolonged sitting causes the spirit and qi to become knotted and obstructed, harming the sinews and vessels. When the sinews and vessels are harmed, the lower back, arms, muscles, and bones become numb.

Prohibition against prolonged thirst.
Prolonged thirst causes the spirit and qi to become parched and scattered, harming the heart. When the heart is harmed, one is prone to panting, palpitations, chest fullness, and counterflow qi.

Prohibition against prolonged gazing.
Prolonged gazing causes the spirit and qi to become disturbed and dissipated, harming the essence. When the essence is harmed, one suffers from seminal emission or listlessness.

Prohibition against prolonged lying down.
Prolonged lying down causes the spirit and qi to accumulate and stagnate, harming the muscles and skin. When the muscles and skin are harmed, one is prone to retching, heart tremors, and shivering.

Prohibition against prolonged listening.
Prolonged listening causes the spirit and qi to become sparse and disordered, harming the will. When the will is harmed, one is prone to forgetfulness, distraction, and spontaneous weeping.

The Lord Lao said: Those who can practice these twelve prohibitions may preserve their essence and qi, gather their spirit and numen, ride the six dragons, gallop through the dark and distant, behold the Nine Capitals, tread the courtyard of the Queen of Earth, mount floating clouds, enter the Great Clarity, lodge in the Void, swiftly traverse with the Six Ding, enter the immortal palaces, and attain longevity.

The forty-five contemplations, as stated in the *Dharma Wheel Sutra*:
If one studies the immortal Dao and contemplates without limit, one may universally attain longevity, and body and spirit may enter wondrous communion.
If one follows a master’s lineage and contemplates without limit, one may universally receive instruction and encouragement, and body and spirit may together uphold the precepts.
If one follows the scriptural teachings and contemplates without limit, one may universally attain wisdom, and body and spirit may together ascend as immortals.
If one attends lectures and contemplates without limit, one may universally gain insight through listening, and body and spirit may together comprehend the profound meaning.
If one observes fasting and precepts and contemplates without limit, one may universally enter the Dharma gate, and body and spirit may together achieve inner and outer purity.
If one sees incense offerings and contemplates without limit, one may universally enter fragrance and flourishing, and body and spirit may avoid treading upon defilement.
If one sees the Three Luminaries and contemplates without limit, darkness and obscurity may universally dissipate, and body and spirit may abide in perpetual light.
If one sees rain pouring and contemplates without limit, one may universally receive its moistening, and body and spirit may attain celestial effusion.
If one sees drifting clouds and contemplates without limit, one may universally receive their covering, and body and spirit may together enter the cloud wheel.
If one sees pristine snow and contemplates without limit, one may universally dwell in purity, and body and spirit may congeal qi into perfection.
If one sees frost and dew and contemplates without limit, one may preserve integrity without decline, and body and spirit may be universally subdued.
If one sees pure water and contemplates without limit, one may universally receive its bathing, and body and spirit may wash away filth and impurity.
If one sees great rivers and contemplates without limit, their vast flow may universally nourish the abyss, and body and spirit may be all-embracing.
If one sees Mount Tai and contemplates without limit, one may universally receive its shelter, and body and spirit may lodge in cliffs and caves.
If one sees trees and contemplates without limit, they may universally remain undecayed, and body and spirit may flourish as dense forests.
If one sees planting and cultivation and contemplates without limit, one may universally attain growth, and body and spirit may daily progress in vitality.
If one sees fruit groves and contemplates without limit, one may universally achieve fruition, and body and spirit may bear fruit and take root.
If one sees flying birds and contemplates without limit, one may universally traverse the sky, and body and spirit may grow feathers and wings in due time.
If one sees birds and beasts and contemplates without limit, one may universally refrain from harmful intent, and body and spirit may seclude themselves to study in secluded forests.
If one sees palaces and towers and contemplates without limit, one may universally gaze upon them in reverence, and body and spirit may ascend to the Wheel-Turning Heavens.
If one sees cities and communities and contemplates without limit, one may universally enter court, and body and spirit may cultivate refinement and adornment.
If one sees terraces and pavilions and contemplates without limit, one may universally behold the eight directions, and body and spirit may perceive the subtle and hidden.
If one sees sacred altars and contemplates without limit, one may universally tread the Dharma gate, and body and spirit may attain the Dao of non-action.
If one sees youths and contemplates without limit, one may universally leave the household life, and body and spirit may together attain the true Dao.
If one sees Daoists and contemplates without limit, one may universally abandon glory and entanglements, and body and spirit may swiftly become flying immortals.
If one sees the virtuous and contemplates without limit, one may universally uphold integrity and righteousness, and body and spirit may together receive transformation.
If one sees sages and contemplates without limit, one may universally attain their Dao, and body and spirit may commune with the true and reach the numinous.
If one sees those in their prime and contemplates without limit, one may universally avoid aging, and body and spirit may long preserve kalpas.
If one sees beautiful women and contemplates without limit, one may transform the body into male form, and body and spirit may guard chastity and enter perfection.
If one sees music and dance and contemplates without limit, one may universally be free from sorrow, and body and spirit may dwell in the silence of the five tones.
If one sees princes and contemplates without limit, one may universally attain ease, and body and spirit may enter the household of the Wheel-Turning King.
If one sees wealth and nobility and contemplates without limit, one may universally attain abundance, and body and spirit may disperse merit and virtue.
If one sees the poor and contemplates without limit, one may universally escape destitution, and body and spirit may transform into joy and peace.
If one sees kitchen and food and contemplates without limit, one may universally attain satiety, and body and spirit may be free from improper flavors.
If one sees level ground and contemplates without limit, one may universally attain tranquility, and body and spirit may cultivate and flourish together.
If one sees houses and dwellings and contemplates without limit, one may universally receive their shelter, and body and spirit may adorn their palaces.
If one retires at dusk and contemplates without limit, one may universally attain peace, and body and spirit may together rest in the empty void.
If one rises at dawn and contemplates without limit, one may universally attain clarity, and body and spirit may together radiate like the sun.
If one dons robes and contemplates without limit, one may universally attain refinement, and body and spirit may together grow feathered garments.
If one wears a headscarf or hat and contemplates without limit, one may universally attain regal crowns, and body and spirit may together don floral canopies.
If one wears sandals or shoes and contemplates without limit, one may universally ride the wind, and body and spirit may tread upon purple clouds.

If one sees a boat and contemplates without limit, one may universally attain crossing, and body and spirit may transcend bridges and beams;
If one sees a chariot and contemplates without limit, one may universally cease walking, and body and spirit may together spur flying wheels;
If one sees a horse and contemplates without limit, one may universally attain flying steps, and body and spirit may ride clouds and mount dragons;
If one sees the Great Clarity and contemplates without limit, one may universally receive emptiness and stillness, and body and spirit may ascend the Golden Portals.

Forty-five contemplations—merit equal to heaven and earth, their number following the Dao of nature.

The Fifty-Two Vows, as stated in the *Sutra of Dispelling Demons*:
If one sees a household with wife and children, may all swiftly escape the prison of love, restrain the mind, and uphold the precepts;
If one sees drinking of wine, may all restrain the gate of life, thereby distancing calamity and chaos;
If one sees a woman of adornment, may all guard the passions and endure desire, aspiring to the worthy;
If one sees a lustful person, may all cast aside perverse thoughts, raising the mind to abstain from transgression;
If one sees a woman, may all endure the severing of frivolity, delighting in the Dao and finding joy therein;
If one sees a chaste person, may all tread the path of faith and right transformation, entering the Dharma-gate on their own;
If one sees the multitude, may all extend benevolence without contention, embracing the Dao and bringing peace to the world;
If one sees a good person, may all at every moment hold them in mind, looking up to follow their Way;
If one sees an evil person, may all forsake malice and turn toward good, never violating the king’s law;
If one sees a poor person, may all diminish the self to bestow kindness, later receiving great fortune;
If one sees a rich person, may all save and aid the myriad beings, receiving blessings for ages to come;
If one sees a noble person, may all receive their teachings and decrees, all conforming to the canons and precepts;
If one sees a lowly person, may all diligently cultivate without slackening, each attaining their proper place;
If one sees the emperor, may all revere and look up to the kingly Way, filial as to father and mother;
If one sees a minister, may all receive their teachings and governance, the four directions turning toward benevolence;
If one sees soldiers and armor, may all each hold a mind of benevolence, the world yielding to the worthy;
If one sees a prince, may all daily sing of great peace, binding the state and inheriting the household;
If one sees a worthy person, may all tread the path of their virtue and power, becoming teachers to the world;
If one sees a young student of sincere mind, may all clearly comprehend the Dharma and its measures, attaining the Dao and becoming immortals;
If one sees a Daoist dwelling in the mountains, may all fully behold the gates of the Dao, swiftly advancing in ascent;
If one sees teaching and transformation, may all personally receive the sagely teachings, all becoming profound and vast;
If one sees a sage, may all revere, serve, and behold them, all nations gazing in unison;
If one sees an immortal, may all perfect the true Dao, flying and ascending to the heavens;
If one sees city walls, may all strictly order and adorn them, taking the Dao as their foundation;
If one sees a great state, may all return to honor and esteem virtue, like rivers flowing to the sea;
If one sees a small state, may all know restraint and emptiness, content in their dwelling;
If one sees a marketplace or court, may all gather like clouds of worthies, all widely propagating the right Dao;
If one sees a tranquil abode, may all accumulate towering merit and virtue, gods and humans attaining their aspirations;
If one sees fasting, precepts, and the burning of incense, may all daily renew their virtue and power, schools distinguishing right from wrong;
If one sees the recitation of scriptures, may all ascend the high seat, all hearing the sagely decrees;
If one sees scriptural teachings, may all each receive and recite them, practicing and universally beholding the rites;
If one sees towers and observatories, may all penetrate and perceive the ten directions, nothing hidden or concealed;
If one sees a lofty mountain, may all establish virtue like it, never again regressing;
If one sees the great sea, may all possess boundless wisdom and thought, aiding in the completion of family and state;
If one rests in a lush grove, may all complete their studies and become masters and teachers, nurturing disciples and spreading the teachings;
If one sees clean hands, may all constantly hold scriptural scrolls, never ceasing for a moment;
If one sees a virtuous mouth, may all delight in chanting the *Dong Jing*, daily renewed without weariness;
If one sees moving feet, may all transcend the Three Realms in a single step, flying and ascending to the Upper Clarity;
If one sees stillness and repose, may all extinguish their light and halt their perfection, abiding in tranquil emptiness;
If one sees food and drink, may all cast off encumbrances and enter purity, preserving the flavor of the Dao;
If one sees dogs and pigs, may all remove their calamities and disasters, acting as fields of blessing;
If one sees illness and disease, may all rely on the Dao to find peace, escaping this bitter affliction;
If one sees death and mourning, may all study the Dao and abide forever, delivering beings from the Three Wretched Paths;
If one sees fishing and hunting, may all not initiate transgression, ultimately entering non-action;
If one sees barbarians, may all be guests and hosts within the Central Kingdom, never born in borderlands;
If one sees the young, may all study in a timely manner, ultimately achieving great renown;
If one sees the old and infirm, may all nurture life through the Dao, never again declining or aging;
If one sees the Three Luminaries, may all universally illuminate with numinous radiance, darkness and obscurity instantly dissolving;
If one sees clouds and rain, may all overflow with beneficent moisture, nothing unsuitable;
If one sees pristine snow, may all constantly dwell in purity and whiteness, carefree and at ease;
If one sees a numinous wind, may all unfold and scatter their inner treasures, virtue flowing near and far;
If one sees pure water, may all wash away filth and attain clarity and emptiness, equalizing the mind;
If one sees fragrant incense, may all receive this flourishing fragrance, all impurities vanishing;
