One must tread with trembling caution, as though crossing thin ice or a deep ravine, and stand with solemn awe, as though before a stern sovereign. When reaching the passages of scripture, one should rise to bow and worship, wholeheartedly invoking goodness, following one’s aspirations in prayer, and ensuring sincerity in suffering—thus one will surely receive a response.

Nowadays, those who establish fasts often fail to attend with due intention. Many neglect the proper observances, chiefly due to the arrogance of great virtue, which leads subordinates into error and negligence. This compilation of precepts is presented to briefly illustrate the necessity of reverence and diligence.

The *Lower Scroll of the Natural Formula* states: To move Heaven and Earth, summon the host of spirits, communicate with the Dao of immortals, penetrate the perfection of the utmost truth, dissolve the sins of past lives, dispel misfortune, repel vengeful foes, cultivate abundant virtue, cure illnesses, and aid all beings—nothing is closer to the fast.

The *Golden Register Simplified Text* says: When conducting a fast, one should appoint one eminent Dharma master, one chief cantor, one fast supervisor, one scripture attendant, one incense attendant, and one lamp attendant. The following day, during the procession, each should be assigned a supervising master, and proclamations should be made widely.

The *Tai Zhen Regulations* states: If following the ritual procedures of fasting and precepts, those of great virtue should walk and sit at the forefront, admonishing their disciples to maintain order. They must ensure that the host harbors no disdain, nor should any engage in contentious debates without fear or restraint. Not only do such actions offend both Daoists and laypeople, the virtuous and the wicked alike, but the ten directions’ great sages also observe in secret. Transgressions invite calamity, and each bears the consequences themselves.

The *Tai Zhen Regulations* further states: When establishing a fast, one should invite upright and incorruptible Daoists—though such individuals are rare. The number invited is not limited, so that even those lacking deep faith may hear of it and cultivate reverence, thereby accumulating merit and virtue, for without the fast, nothing can be accomplished. Truly, the upright and incorruptible are worthy of reverence, while the greedy and lustful, filled with self-importance, always seek precedence in offerings. If, after selection, they fail to examine themselves and instead become a great affliction to the host’s merit-making, they should reflect and withdraw—only then are they worthy of being called disciples of the Dao.

The *Golden Register Simplified Text* says: The household establishing the fast should, one day prior, bathe and cleanse both inner and outer spaces. All family members, young and old, should unite in mind for the fast.

The *Tai Zhen Regulations* states: Once the fast officials arrive for the overnight stay, they should all bathe in fragrant water. Afterward, they shall enter the Dharma hall to receive the precepts and instructions, maintaining solemn and orderly decorum. Violating any rule incurs a penalty of one stroke. The regulations state: Disobeying the Dharma master and acting on private whims incurs a penalty of two strokes. The regulations further state: Those who fail to arrive the night before may not enter the fast. People today often neglect the procession the following morning, yet if they arrive the night before for the preliminary rites, how can they claim sincerity? Some may seek external distractions, or sleep in their chambers, and when roles are assigned, omissions arise. The next day, during the incense procession, confusion and disputes erupt, all because the Dharma master failed to announce the procedures the night before. Such disorder leads to: first, the underworld recording transgressions; second, disrupting the Dharma rites; third, eroding reverence and faith; and fourth, rendering merit and goodness unreliable.

A treatise states: Those who do evil openly are punished by people; those who do evil in secret are punished by spirits. Only when one is clear before both people and spirits can one act with true independence. Lord Lao said: The net of Heaven is vast, its mesh wide but never failing. Since the host is offering rites and provisions, how can one sit idly and incur calamity? Let each reflect and awaken to this truth.

The *Tai Zhen Regulations* states: Once fast officials receive the proclamations, they may not converse with outsiders, or they incur a penalty of two strokes. The regulations state: On the day of the fast, one must maintain purity, focus the spirit on the Dao, mark the gate to bar guests, and separate inner and outer spaces.

The *Thousand Perfected Regulations* says: Three days before establishing a fast, the household should cleanse both inner and outer areas. At the gate, erect a long banner to signal to guests that those indulging in meat, garlic, or leeks may not enter, while those of reverence and faith may approach with solemnity.

The *Cantor’s Notes* states: When leading the fast procession, chanting and recitation come first. One must know the proper etiquette of progression and set the schedule for morning and evening.

The *Ritual Decorum for Spreading the Fast* says: The cantor’s duty is to clarify the correct scriptures, define roles, and oversee the timing of processions and the decorum of recitation. First, the Dharma drum is sounded, then the assembly is led forth. The cantor upholds the ritual standards, relying on chanting to guide reverence. When establishing a fast, the cantor must be capable and discerning: first, consulting with the Dharma master; second, understanding the host’s intent for the fast; third, setting the schedule in advance; and fourth, striking the drum and ringing the bell. In consulting the Dharma master, one must follow their guidance on progression and retreat, engaging in discussion rather than acting on personal whim or unilaterally controlling the disciples. In understanding the host’s intent, if the fast is for joy and celebration, the decorum should please the assembly; if for illness or hardship, diligence is paramount. One must predetermine the number of processions for the three periods or six periods.

The *Golden Register Simplified Text* states: The six periods for processions are dawn, midday, sunset, evening rest, midnight, and cockcrow. The three periods are dawn, midday, and evening rest alone.

The *Tai Zhen Regulations* says: All fasts held for others are conducted in three periods to alleviate suffering, rescue the distressed, repent sins, and dispel calamities. For seeking blessings, one recites and prays day and night without rest. If fatigue accumulates over days, one may rest in shifts, but scripture recitation and precept teachings must continue unceasingly, with processions conducted uniformly, never deficient.

If the officiating priest falls ill, report it, and the assembly shall withdraw from the ritual hall. The ritual space must remain solemn; one may not convalesce within it. Those recovering from illness shall be housed in separate quarters, lest they disturb the ritual space. If, by fortune, one is able to remain in the ritual, ascend the hall with dignity, refraining from drowsiness. Discreetly encourage one another, without resorting to punishment, for uneven penalties disrupt the utmost sincerity of prayer. Regularly apply the statutes of reprimand and discipline to ensure uniformity within and without, guiding the benighted toward enlightenment. Masters must not indiscriminately punish disciples; disciples, when punished, may not protest. If the punishment is indeed unjust, they may humbly appeal, but only to express their true intent. Harsh words must not be spoken, for they breed resentment. Master and disciple share the discourse of the Five Phases.

During the three-period processions, after the midday recess, rest until the evening meal; after the midnight recess, rest until cockcrow. At all other times, ascend to lecture and recite scriptures. During the six-period processions, after the midday recess, rest until the evening meal; after the dusk recess, rest until the hour of quietude; after the midnight recess, rest until cockcrow. Rest during these three periods, while at all other times, ascend to lecture and recite scriptures. Let heart and gaze converge, the mind fixed upon the mysterious void. In every action and deed, adhere strictly to the ritual codes.

When striking the drum or sounding the bell to summon immortals and gather sages, there are two kinds of ritual drums: one is the knocking of teeth, the other the golden bell and jade chime. As for the knocking of teeth, the *Ritual Codes* states: first sound the ritual drum, then lead the assembly. The ritual drum may be struck three, seven, nine, ten, twelve, twenty-four, thirty-six, forty-nine, seventy-two, or eighty-one times, as the occasion demands. The *Preface to the Scripture of Lord Lao* says: knock the teeth thrice, swallow saliva thrice. The *Scripture of Ascending the Mystery* states: knock the teeth seven times for the Seven Luminaries, for the Dao’s clarity must be awakened. The *Chapter on Generating the Spirit* says: knock the teeth nine times. The *Scripture of Ascending the Mystery* also states: knock the teeth twelve times, then exhale thrice. Elsewhere, it says: knock the teeth twenty-four times. The *Scripture of Salvation* says: knock the teeth thirty-two times, to be heard in the thirty-two heavens. The *Scripture of Ascending the Mystery* states: the Celestial Master, facing west, knocks the teeth forty-nine times; the Most High, facing east, knocks the teeth seventy-two times. It further says: the Most High knocks the teeth eighty-one times. The *Scripture of the Eight Secret Sayings of the Upper Clarity* states: the method of knocking the teeth—left against left is called "breaking the Heavenly Bell"; right against right is called "shaking the Heavenly Chime"; upper and lower center teeth striking each other is called "sounding the Heavenly Drum." If suddenly encountering inauspicious omens, strike the Heavenly Bell thirty-six times. For exorcising evil and invoking divine might, strike the Heavenly Chime. For meditating upon the Dao, summoning the true, and calling forth spirits, sound the Heavenly Drum. Though the act of knocking the teeth is singular, it comprises three distinct methods. Thus, evil flees at the sound of the Heavenly Bell; mountain spirits are subdued by the resonance of the Heavenly Chime; and spirits are summoned by the reverberation of the Heavenly Drum. The notes must be measured, the subtle inner resonance felt. One cannot seek uniformity nor rely on chance to summon them.

The *Records of Fengdu* recounts: Bao Zhu, a man of Jibei, over forty years of age and without any Daoist arts, suffered a stroke that twisted his mouth and face, causing his teeth to chatter incessantly. At the age of one hundred and twenty-seven, he perished in a great flood, swept away by the River of Longevity. The Northern Emperor repeatedly dispatched demon-slayers to seize him, but the demons dared not approach. When the demon officials inquired as to the reason, the slayers replied: "This man possesses many arts to restrain us. He constantly knocks his teeth, sounding the Heavenly Drum to alert his body’s spirits. The spirits do not scatter, and demonic qi cannot enter."

As for the second kind, the bell and chime, the *Tai Zhen Regulations* states: before the ritual hall and upon the scripture platform, hang a golden bell and jade chime, to be sounded at the appointed times. Before processions and lectures, strike them first, not only to alert the assembly but also to move the host of spirits. When humans and spirits are thus connected, they gather in unison, magnifying the Dao and aiding beings, their abundant virtue converging as one. It further states: when the Dharma-body strikes the bell, one must be girded in solemn attire, the mind focused on the spirit, as if meeting one’s own true self. Strike the mallet evenly, beginning softly and increasing to full resonance, the number of strikes—twelve, twenty-four, or thirty-six—determined by the size of the assembly.

The *Scripture of the Royal One’s Former Deeds* says: when sounding the bell, don the proper Dharma-robes, approach the bell first, and take up the mallet. Recite the prayer: "The round mallet strikes the Dharma-bell, its sound flowing through the ten directions. Below, it penetrates the long night; above, it resounds in the jade chambers. The Nine Dark Realms hear and escape suffering; the Seven Ancestors ascend to the immortal halls." Strike thrice, for the yin number arises from two and is perfected in ten. Flick the bell ten times with the nail, producing the sound of long fangs, for the yang number arises from one and is perfected in nine. Then strike nine times, evoking the resonance of jade and jasper. The three strikes ascend to the Three Heavens—Clear Tenuity, Jade Clarity, and Great Redness; they resonate with the Three Realms—Formless, Form, and Desire; and they warn the Three Destinies—hell-dwellers, hungry ghosts, and animals. Three Heavens, Three Realms, Three Destinies—three threes make nine, hence nine strikes. Conclude with the "Gathering the Long Dharma," twenty-seven strikes: nine above for the Nine Heavenly Lords of the Dao, nine in the middle for the Nine True Emperors of the Nine Palaces, and nine below to summon the suffering souls of the Nine Dark Realms. Three final strikes, totaling thirty-six, ascend to the Thirty-Six Heavenly Emperors, resonate with the Thirty-Six Revered Scriptures, and penetrate the Thirty-Six Earthly Gates. If performed correctly, the left immortal youth records good deeds; if flawed, the right jade maiden records transgressions. Each is clearly noted, and violations incur hidden retribution.

The *Laws of the Mysterious Capital* states: if the chief lecturer fails to announce the proceedings, they shall self-impose a penalty of one catty of incense.

Now, those who have left the household life yet do not adhere to the regulated striking of the bell—entrusting the matter to subordinates while the master and preceptor lack proper standards—commit errors not only in the improper wearing of robes and footwear but also in the misalignment of tempo and rhythm. First, such conduct brings shame upon those who witness it; second, it distorts the mirror of discipline. Thus, diagrams and models are provided for others to follow, that future generations may have a rule to emulate.

*The Supervisor’s Manual for Vegetarian Feasts* states: The Dao is made manifest through sagely figures, and blessings are entrusted to human propagation. Without correction and reproof, there can be no adherence to canonical statutes.

*The Ritual Dignity for Vegetarian Feasts* says: One must earnestly encourage the negligent, reprove transgressions, and rectify faults—without partiality or favoritism, concealment or excess. Upholding the canonical regulations, one must ensure fairness and propriety.

For all ritual affairs depend upon the right persons. Without mutual refinement, how can precedents be established? One must adhere to the Dharma and not indulge personal sentiment.

*The Scripture of Ascending the Mystery* enumerates ten duties of the feast supervisor:
1. Instruct the host offering the feast to submit a formal request to the Dharma master three days prior, stating their aspirations, the location of the ritual, the names of those invited to the feast, and their Dao ranks. The Dharma master, upon receiving the request, shall immediately transcribe it and submit it to the supervisory officials.
2. Instruct the host to return home and inform those invited to the feast that the request has been submitted. They should each purify themselves, avoid sharing beds or seats with laypersons, and refrain from all evil and unwholesome conduct. From this point until the feast’s conclusion, they must guard their body, speech, and mind, adhering strictly to the precepts of the Most High.
3. Instruct the host not to steal offerings meant for spirits or purchase prepared food to serve the feast participants.
4. Instruct the host, upon encountering unusual persons who wish to attend the feast and listen to the scriptures, not to calculate the cost of food and drink or obstruct their participation.
5. Instruct the host, after submitting the request, to return home and burn incense and light lamps in a quiet chamber or the feast hall to await the supervisory officials, who shall descend to inspect.
6. Instruct the host not to decline the feast’s responsibilities or fail to provide for guests.
7. Instruct the host to arrange for the distribution of alms after the feast, according to their means—even a single coin or thread suffices to express sincerity.
8. Instruct the host not to wear loose belts or skirts as if serving as an attendant.
9. Instruct the host not to welcome the master upon arrival yet fail to see them off upon departure, for such inconsistency betrays a divided heart and forgetfulness of duty.
10. Instruct the host not to use the leftovers from the post-feast distribution to absolve spiritual debts, for this is greed and stinginess, lacking reverence and solemnity.

*The Laws of the Mysterious Capital* states: If the host, whether within or without, fails to inspect and reprove, or if their voice is loud and harsh, they shall be penalized five *sheng* of oil. If the host’s provisions are lacking, they shall be penalized two *sheng* of oil. If the supervisor, upon witnessing a fault, fails to reprove it due to private bias, they shall be penalized six *sheng* of oil. If they observe a deficiency but do not report it for supplementation, they shall be penalized three *sheng* of oil. If a feast participant engages in frivolous or vulgar speech, they shall be penalized one catty of incense, five *sheng* of oil, and three *liang* of cinnabar.

The statutes further decree: If one violates ritual dignity and refuses reproof, they shall be reported to the Three Treasures and expelled from the feast. If the reproof is unjust, the accuser shall be punished accordingly. If the presiding Dharma master becomes aware of their own fault, they must confess and rectify it; if they do not, the supervisor shall reprove them according to the Dharma. If a ritual is flawed, from the first to the third offense, the supervisor shall impose penalties accordingly. For offenses beyond the third, merits shall be revoked, and the transgressor shall be publicly denounced before their peers, barred from the Dharma seat. If the supervisor condones misconduct without reproof, they shall share the same penalty.

*The Attendant’s Manual for Incense* states: Water washes away dust, and incense dispels impurity. Through these hundred harmonies, trust is conveyed to the Three Clarities.

*The Ritual Dignity for Vegetarian Feasts* says: The duty of the incense attendant is to maintain the brazier with solemn order, ensuring fragrant smoke arises continuously. Let the smoke flow through the night and the fire burn through the day—this is the foremost task of ritual affairs, to be observed diligently at dawn and dusk.

*The Scripture of Fundamental Boundaries* states: Scatter celestial incense and flowers like falling snow, their fragrance delighting the hearts of all. It further says: Burn hundred-fold harmonies, the revered wind-borne incense, and scatter myriad varieties of celestial tree blossoms.

*The Scripture of Many Chapters* says: Fragrance flies to eight convergences, like lightning flashing and beacons rising. It further says: Golden youths waft incense, and lunar palaces scatter flowers.

*The Fixed Resolve* states: Hundred-fold treasured incense, though unburned, perfumes all.

*The Scripture of Ascending the Mystery* says: The fragrance of incense, sweet and pure, permeates the eight directions.

*The Preface to the Five Talismans* says: Burn hundred-fold incense to dispel impurity. It further says: Burn incense to invoke the Five Emperors, observing the direction of the fragrant smoke. If the smoke rises straight to the center, the Yellow Emperor descends first. If it flows eastward, the Blue Emperor descends first. If it flows southward, the Red Emperor descends first. If it flows westward, the White Emperor descends first. If it flows northward, the Black Emperor descends first.

*The Laws of the Mysterious Capital* states: After the morning and evening prostrations, burning incense on that day will extinguish calamities. Violations of this law incur a penalty of one *ji* of reckoning.

*The Laws of the Mysterious Capital* further states: If the incense fire is overturned, the penalty is two catties of incense. If the incense smoke is interrupted, the penalty is four catties of oil. If one rushes about while burning incense, the penalty is two catties of oil. If one handles incense without washing hands or rinsing the mouth, the penalty is two catties of oil.

*The Hidden Instructions for Ascending to Perfection* says: Incense is what celestial perfected beings use to communicate, and earthly spirits rely upon it to convey words. Thus, when praying or meditating, one must burn it to the left and right. This smoke can illuminate the mysterious and convey intent, and there are those who attend and protect, proclaiming the sincerity of the words. It further says: In establishing cities, counties, and high mounds, burning a single stone until it turns red ensures perpetual preservation. Burning incense with one *jiu* of this stone is truly economical and essential.

*The Attendant’s Manual for Lamps* states: Upon entering seclusion for a feast, lamps and candles take precedence, illuminating the eight gates and shining through the nine nights. Once the merit of continuous brightness is established, the blessings of enduring vigil may be cultivated.

*The Ritual Dignity of the Feast* states: Tending the lamps is one’s duty. The orchid-oil lamp gleams at dusk, binding brightness to the western sun; its flames blaze upon the altar ground, illuminating the pure night. Should rain arise, temporarily cease; if wind blows, briefly extinguish. Host and guest shall deliberate together, adapting to the occasion’s rise or decline—never suppress or sever, lest the ritual’s completion be diminished.

*The Hidden Instructions for Ascending to Perfection* says: The Perfected summon the radiance of the sun to illuminate, and command the essence of the moon to brighten the obscure.

*The Bright Code of the Four Poles* states: On the days of *Lichun* and the Spring Equinox, kindle nine lamps in the central courtyard; on *Lixia* and the Summer Solstice, kindle three lamps; on *Liqiu* and the Autumn Equinox, kindle seven lamps; on *Lidong* and the Winter Solstice, kindle five lamps; on one’s *benming* day, kindle twelve lamps. At midnight, expose the lamps beneath the open sky—facing east in spring, south in summer, west in autumn, north in winter, and toward *Tai Sui* on one’s *benming* day. Knock the teeth twenty-four times and pray:

"Supreme Perfected Ones, Ten Thousand Sage Emperors, Jade Lords of the Five Thearchs, and the Assembly of Immortal Overseers—today is auspicious and fine. The Eight Nodal Days unfold in array. Yang severs, Yin is examined, and the Nine Yin are extinguished. From this moment forth, may the Seven Mysteries be released, escaping the realm of fire, forever departing the Mountain of Knives. The Three Paths and Five Sufferings shall not burden this body. May I be as one with Heaven and Earth, eternally safeguarded by the Celestial Dawn. May the Five Vows and Eight Assemblies be fulfilled without fail."

Upon completion, remove the headscarf and bow one hundred and twenty times, pressing the forehead to the ground. Then, don the scarf again, face the heavens, and reflect: "Today, this body receives the grace of Heaven’s favor, bestowed with form and bones, to be reborn as a flying immortal." Swallow twenty-four breaths of *qi* while gazing upward. After three years of such practice, all past transgressions shall be eradicated. Body and Perfected shall become one, untouched by the Five Sufferings, escaping the realm of fire, attaining the rank of Perfected, and roaming the Jade Chambers in revelry. This is a wondrous formula—guard it well and do not lightly disclose it.

*The Golden Register’s Simplified Writs* says: To deliver beings from life and death, establish a feast in the central courtyard. The earthen altar shall be square in breadth. The upper altar measures twenty-four *chi*, the lower thirty-two *chi*, with four sides, four corners, and ten gates above and below, each gate five *chi* wide. Adorn with railings and inscribed plaques, modeling Heaven and Earth. At the altar’s center, place a long lampstand, nine *chi* in height, upon which nine lamps are kindled, their light shining upward to the Nine Heavens and downward to the Nine Netherworlds. Beyond the gates on all four sides, place nine lamps each, totaling thirty-six lamps to encircle the altar.

*The Golden Register’s Simplified Writs* states: Upon entering the hall for seclusion, once the lamps are lit, bow three times toward them and recite the incantation:

"True Unity’s yang radiance, flames of crimson smoke ascend. Twelve Lads of Illuminating Light, penetrating this dwelling and this body. A hundred evils shattered, ten thousand ghosts slain. Brilliance shines clear, communicating with the Perfected and reaching the numinous. Ascending into formlessness, merging with the Dao’s spontaneity."

Upon completion, withdraw from the hall. To extinguish the lamps, recite the incantation:

"Mysterious Yin seals the light, the long lamp continues to shine. The Great Yang has dawned, the Eight Might open clarity. The lamp connects to the Great Yin, its light clinging to the flourishing scene. The Two Perfected of Sun and Moon unite with me."

Then extinguish the flame.

On the *benming* day, kindle a lamp to illuminate the Seven *Po*; on the *xingnian* day, kindle a lamp to illuminate the Three *Hun*; on the *Tai Sui* day, kindle a lamp to illuminate the whole body; on the *da mu* day, kindle a lamp to illuminate the decayed remains; on the *xiao mu* day, kindle a lamp to illuminate the Great Yin; before the hall, kindle seven lamps to illuminate the Seven Generations; in the central courtyard, kindle nine lamps to illuminate the Nine Netherworlds; beside the gates, kindle two lamps to illuminate the palace and dwelling; at the Earth Gate, kindle twenty-four lamps to illuminate the Twenty-Four Vital *Qi*; in the eight directions, kindle eight lamps to illuminate the Eight Trigrams; at the center, kindle nine lamps to illuminate the Nine Palaces; at the Heavenly Gate, kindle ten lamps to illuminate the Ten Directions; kindle twenty-eight lamps to illuminate the Twenty-Eight Constellations; kindle thirty-two lamps to illuminate the Thirty-Two Heavens; kindle five lamps to illuminate the Five Marchmounts.

*The Golden Register’s Simplified Writs* says: The ritual dignity of kindling lamps is of utmost merit, illuminating all Heavens above and brightening all Earth below. For eight directions and nine nights, all shall behold the radiant light. The attendants of the lamps must apply diligent care, ensuring the lamps’ glow persists through the night.

*The Ritual of the Luminous Perfected* states: In the central courtyard, kindle nine lamps upon a pole to illuminate the Nine Netherworlds. On the four sides of the altar, kindle nine lamps each, totaling thirty-six lamps. Expose the scriptures in the central courtyard, circumambulate the Nine Netherworlds, lamps burning and incense wafting, as the Dao is revered and repentance made.

*The Yellow Register’s Simplified Writs* says: Kindle ninety spirit lamps.

*The Bright Perfected Code* states: In times of disaster or urgency, inscribe five talismans of the Perfected Writs in cinnabar upon the central courtyard, placing one in each of the five directions. Offer five *liang* of gold and five dragon servants to stabilize the five talismans, and five-colored silken pledges to secure the Five Thearchs. In spring, kindle nine lamps—or ninety, or nine hundred; in summer, kindle three lamps—or thirty, or three hundred; in autumn, kindle seven lamps—or seventy, or seven hundred; in winter, kindle five lamps—or fifty, or five hundred; in the months of the four seasons, kindle twelve lamps—or one hundred twenty, or one thousand two hundred. At the center, erect a long pole and kindle nine lamps upon it.

*The Statutes of the Mysterious Capital* says: If the lamp or fire is extinguished while tending, offer one *jin* of incense; if the lamp or candle is overturned, forfeit five *sheng* of oil.

*Essential Rituals and Precepts for Cultivation*, Scroll Eight
*Essential Rituals and Precepts for Cultivation*, Scroll Nine

Jurist of the Three Caverns, Zhu Faman, *On Robes and Attire*

To transcend the dust-laden world and take refuge within the fortress of the Dao, one must diligently cultivate morning and night, forsaking the crooked to return to the upright. Only with pure ritual vestments may one pay homage to the Perfected; how can one approach the sacred with soiled or defiled garments?

Thus, the coarse robe mirrors the sun and moon, while the mantle embodies the raiment of qi and destiny. In all comings and goings, one must uphold reverence and dignity. A single lapse by one may corrupt the entire assembly of practitioners, and in this age of frivolity and deceit, from where shall we draw our standards?

Yet in these times, some treat their pure robes with careless disdain, or lend their ritual vestments to others. They sit upon the ground and stain them with mud, or use them as mats in place of felt. Such conduct leaves the divine youths without protection and evokes gasps of dismay from both Daoists and laity. Even a disheveled turban or cap brings reproach upon those nearby—how much more so when one dons the robes of the Dao while behaving like a monkey or ape!

*The Statutes of the Thousand Perfected* states: The demeanor of Daoist assemblies must be solemn and orderly. Robes must be kept immaculate, and conduct must be exemplary, so that good intentions arise in the world and the heavens themselves sing their praises. But if one’s appearance is slovenly and displeasing to the assembly, one is unfit to receive offerings, bringing disgrace upon the Dao and its teachings. This is a failing of the precious Dao and severs the seed of great compassion. Yet if one’s conduct and bearing inspire admiration, the precious Dao shines forth, and the gateway of the Dao finds its rightful place.

The statutes further decree: The standard attire consists of a long Chinese robe with wide sleeves, distinct from secular dress. One must not wear the same garments as laypeople—no fur-lined robes, leather boots, or horn-handled utensils. All implements must be plain and lacquered, never adorned with gold, silver, or bronze. When engaging in ritual practice or paying homage to the host of sages, one must don the proper vestments of dignity, arranged according to rank. For *Shangqing* practitioners, robes are purple; for *Lingbao*, they are yellow; for *Sanwu*, they are crimson. When not performing rituals, robes may be of undyed yellow cloth, but one must not wear brocade, damask, or embroidered silks to dazzle the vulgar. Female officiants, moreover, must not adorn themselves with gold or jade hairpins or bracelets, nor with floral patterns or cosmetics—first, because such things offend the Dao, and second, because they betray a lack of reverence.

*The Statutes of the Supreme Perfected* states: Do not covet fine, smooth, ornate, or rare fabrics, nor precious jeweled crowns or tassels. One should wear only the proper vestments of the Dao, coarse and simple. They must be clean enough to cover the body but not overly large; sufficient to ward off the cold but not excessively thick.

Should a vestment have been laundered thrice, it must then be given to another.

*The Statutes of the Four Luminaries* states: The ritual mantle should measure four feet and nine inches in width, corresponding to the number of the four seasons; five feet and five inches in length, aligning with the qi of Heaven and Earth. Its lining is blue, its outer layer purple. It must comprise twenty-four complete inner strips and fifteen complete outer strips, totaling thirty-nine strips inside and out, to correspond to the thirty-nine stations of the Perfected Emperors. Thus, when one’s person bears the insignia of the Emperor Sovereign, one is fit to don the vestments of the Emperor Sovereign. Without such vestments, one must not rashly handle the sacred scriptures. Should one chant even a single line, the sound will resound through the Nine Heavens, the Ten Thousand Emperors will descend to their thrones, and the Nine Perfected will take their places—such resonance is not to be taken lightly. To recite scriptures in a single-layered robe invites demonic intrusion, the clamor of myriad spirits, the loss of one’s spirit, and the dispersal of one’s qi. Death will consign one to the Dark Springs, where the hun and po will endure hardship, barred from entry into immortality.

Those who possess such ritual vestments with their august insignia are attended by twelve jade lads and twelve jade maidens, who guard and serve the Perfected. These vestments must not be lent to others, nor defiled by impurity or disrespect. Even when encountering worldly dignitaries, one must not converse with them from a seated position—how much less, then, when paying homage to the Perfected and the Divine Sovereigns, should one presume to address them from a seat. To do so incurs a hundred days of unseen punishment. If one meets another on the road without a mat, one may perform obeisance on the ground. When prostrating on the ground, the hands should reach the earth, the fingers bent so that the backs of the fingers touch the ground, preventing dust from soiling the palms—this is called "guarding purity." This is the "Fourfold Illumination of the Heart" prostration.

It is further said: An *Ascended Mystery* Dharma Master may perform the "prostration of the heart" at dawn and dusk, without needlessly subjecting the body to wind and dust. At the six periods of the day—dawn, noon, sunset, nightfall, midnight, and cockcrow—one should sit upright facing east, west, south, or north, closing the eyes and clicking the teeth, as if in the presence of the ritual assembly. Turning the mind toward each direction in contemplation, one prostrates in thought and word, which suffices to summon the descent of the Heavenly Perfected.

*The Statutes of Illuminating the Perfected* states: The Dao of great compassion is a boundless field of merit. Bowing in reverence, knocking the head to the ground, and offering words of humility and gratitude—when the cinnabar heart and intent are fully engaged, and sincerity is complete, the response will be naturally felt. If the body is weary and unable to prostrate fully, one may perform the "prostration of the heart" to the Heavenly Perfected, sparing oneself the strain of physical submission. To feign external devotion while the heart is negligent is merely to exhaust the body in ritual without purpose. For those of true faith, when heart and mouth align, the offering of incense and the vow of reverence ascend to the various heavens, dissolving both the sins of this life and the karmic retributions of past lives. This applies to the rites for the elderly and infirm.

The young and able, however, often hear these precepts yet pay them no heed. First, this breeds arrogance; second, it fails to set an example for others; third, it disregards the diligence and effort required; and fourth, it reflects a lack of faith and reverence.

*The Yellow Register Simplified Writs* states: During the act of prostration, one bows nine times to the east, twelve times to the southeast, three times to the south, twelve times to the southwest, seven times to the west, twelve times to the northwest, five times to the north, twelve times to the northeast, twelve times to the nadir, thirty-two times to the zenith, three times to the Palace of the Sun, seven times to the Palace of the Moon, nine times to the constellations, and twice to the Five Peaks. This is but a brief outline for a single retreat; all retreats rely on prostration as their essential practice. Each should be performed with upright posture—how else can one manifest dignity and decorum?

*The Scripture on Stabilizing the Will* states: To knock the head to the ground until blood flows in great quantity—even this must not cease. Such is the practice for the young and able. Whether sitting or rising, one must adhere to the prescribed forms.

In the pursuit of the Dao and the veneration of the Perfected, the order of precedence, movement, sitting, leaning, and standing must follow canonical decorum. Without proper rank and position, the ritual order falls into disarray.

*The Statutes of the Thousand Perfected* states: When entering an assembly and sitting or rising, all must adhere to the proper forms. When sitting, men should first place the left foot forward, aligning the shoe near the edge of the platform, then bring the right foot forward, gently pressing the hands against the platform. When rising, first lower the left foot to retrieve the shoe, then the right, standing with measured composure. Women should first place the right foot forward.

When entering the hall, first stand outside the door to adjust one’s garments, tablet, robe, and mantle. Face east, gather the ritual implements, and contemplate the assembly within as if it were the Ten Directions’ Heavenly Worthies, the host of Perfected who have attained the Dao, the present king, and the prefectural and county magistrates. When entering the hall, if there are two doors—east and west—enter from the side corresponding to one’s seat. If seated to the west of the door, enter from the west; if seated to the east, enter from the east. One must not cross paths at the doorway.

*The Statutes of the Mysterious Capital* states: Daoist priests and nuns, when attending or departing from a retreat, must inform the retreat supervisor and bow once to the Dharma Master, then bow three times to the scripture hall before leaving. Upon returning, the same forms apply. Violations incur a penalty of one catty of incense. If one sits improperly in the retreat hall, the penalty is half a tael of cinnabar. If one sits or rises without grace and measured steps, the penalty is one catty of incense. If one moves out of turn, the penalty is half a catty of incense. If the chanting of auspicious verses is not in unison, the penalty is five sheng of oil. If one sits or rises without proper bowing and yielding, the penalty is half a catty of incense.

The statutes further state: If, during a retreat, one leaves for personal matters and fails to return, and this is reported by the assembly, the penalty is one sheng of oil. If, while conducting the ritual, one speaks with outsiders, the penalty is one catty of incense. If one’s hair is unbound or one walks hastily, the penalty is one catty of incense.

The statutes state: A *Great Cavern* Dharma Master must not share a meal with laypeople, for to do so offends the spirits of the body. Violations incur fifty days of unseen illness.

*The Golden Register Simplified Writs* states: When attending a retreat and arranging seating, the decorum for sitting and rising is as follows: If the Dharma Master’s seat is in the west, he faces east, while the disciples’ seats are arranged to the south, facing north. If the Dharma Master’s seat is in the south, he faces north, while the disciples’ seats are arranged to the east, facing west. If the Dharma Master’s seat is in the east, he faces west, while the disciples’ seats are arranged to the north, facing south.

If the Dharma Master’s seat is in the north, he faces south, while the disciples’ seats are arranged to the west, facing east. The Dharma Master and disciples must not sit on the same side.

It further states: In sitting and rising, precedence is determined by rank. If ranks are equal, precedence is given to seniority in age; if ages are equal, precedence is given to virtue and power.

It further states: When observing the decorum for sitting and rising, if it is time to rise, the disciples must first descend and stand to the west, facing the master, before stepping down from their seats. When returning, the master first ascends to his seat, and the disciples take their places, bow once, and then sit.

*The Yellow Register Simplified Writs* states: In the decorum for revering the scriptures, when entering or leaving the scripture chamber, one must always bow toward the scripture hall upon arrival and departure, announcing one’s movements. Violations are adjudicated by the Supervisory Office.

*The Sutra of Esoteric Teachings* states: In the order of sitting and rising, the five qi are arranged as follows: first, the upper central qi; next, the orthodox governance; then, the inner governance; then, the separate governance and dispersed qi; then, the Register of One Hundred Fifty Generals; then, the Register of Seventy-Five Generals; then, the tally of Ten Generals; then, the youths; then, the immortal officials; then, the upper numinous officials; and finally, the night watchmen. If those receiving the teachings are of the same rank, precedence is given to those who received it earlier, with later recipients ranked below. Men and women must not be intermingled. If those wearing the registers of generals of the same rank received them on the same day, precedence is determined by the order in which they embraced the Dao.

*The Hidden Instructions for Ascending to Perfection* states: If men and women share the same mat to sit or rest, their offenses are recorded in the Northern Mystery. If they commit defilement, calamity befalls their three generations of ancestors.

*The Decorum of the Ascending Mystery* states: A Dharma Master must not sit in a seat lower than others, except when compelled by superior power. When an Ascending Mystery Dharma Master sits or rises, he must defer to his original master, except when receiving the teachings. An Ascending Mystery Dharma Master must not associate or interact with base and vulgar people, except when traveling together for the practice of the Dao.

*The Sutra of the Ascending Mystery* states: During retreat assemblies and the practice of the Dao, no Orthodox Unity Daoist may share the same mat with an Highest Clarity Great Cavern Dharma Master. A Highest Clarity Great Cavern Dharma Master may not share the same mat with a Five Chapters Numinous Treasure Dharma Master, nor transmit or exchange clothing and items. A Numinous Treasure Five Chapters Dharma Master, in turn, may not share the same mat with an Ascending Mystery Esoteric Teaching Dharma Master.

*The Ritual for Mourning Attire of Alliance Dharma Masters* states: In receiving the teachings, one progresses from lower to higher ranks, each with its proper order. For those who receive the registers of governance, the Five Natural Methods of Lord Lao, the Middle Alliance, the Great Alliance, and Highest Clarity, Highest Clarity is the ultimate rank. There is no mourning attire prescribed for the Ascending Mystery, so they follow the Ascending Mystery attire system. For the Great Alliance and Highest Clarity, a one-evening mourning period is observed. The duration of mourning clearly reflects the hierarchy. Thus, it is evident that, according to the *Sutra of the Ascending Mystery* alone, it is uniquely esteemed as the highest.

*Supervising the Retreat Manual* states: When attending a retreat and partaking in meals, one must be meticulous in conduct. Even in handling broth and holding bowls, one must maintain decorum. When holding chopsticks and spoons, all movements must be measured and deliberate.

*The Sutra of the Ascending Mystery* states: The Most High commanded the Supervisor of the Lingyang Kitchen to inspect meals according to ten principles: first, reflect on the grace of the Dao; second, reflect on the grace of the master; third, reflect on the assembly as a field of blessed merit...

Fourth, reflect on the host’s discernment in distinguishing truth from falsehood, and their ability to provide for the worthy and good.

Fifth, rejoice in being counted among the retinue, fully partaking in the Dao’s current.

Sixth, reflect on the host’s earnest intent in offering the meal; do not allow others to lightly criticize its quality or reject it out of dislike.

Seventh, if the host provides food that is not in accordance with the Dao, do not allow it to be served.

Eighth, if anyone among the assembly requests food that is not in accordance with the Dao, immediately dismiss them and exclude them from the meal.

Ninth, ensure that all, from the highest to the lowest, observe proper order and sequence.

Tenth, do not fear eating later; adjustments may be made as needed.

*The Regulations of the Thousand Perfected* states: During an ordinary retreat, one supervisor of the kitchen shall first inspect the head cook, ensuring that fruits, vegetables, and seasonings are properly prepared, and that the flavors of raw and cooked dishes are well-balanced. Next, they shall observe the time by the gnomon’s shadow, or by the water clock if the sky is overcast.

At the proper time for the retreat meal, when the hour arrives, the bell is rung to summon the assembly. Each person brings their eating utensils to the refectory and arranges them according to their seated position. Once the utensils are set and the bell is silenced, the ritual of circumambulation and invocation begins. After the invocation concludes, all sit in their places. Water is passed once around the assembly, and then the meal begins. Order is maintained according to rank, with the first offering placed before the master. The kitchen supervisor kneels and presents the dish before the master’s seat. A single strike of the chime signals the assembly, and the senior adept recites the invocation:

*"This retreat is established for the Perfected of the Dao, for the seeds of the Dao, the fathers and mothers of the Dao, the Perfected, the Divine, the Immortals, and the Sages. Together we cultivate the Dao and partake in this meal, that we may attain boundless longevity."*

When the invocation ends, the dish is placed before the master’s seat, followed by the meal for the retreat officials. The server softly announces the names of each dish—fruits, vegetables, and grains—so that the assembly may receive them with their utensils. If one does not wish to partake, they should not take from the senior adept. All should observe the food as it is passed, completing the full circuit. Another strike of the chime signals: *"Please begin the meal."* The assembly sits in solemn order, without speech or laughter, to partake.

While eating, one must not smack their lips, make sounds of enjoyment, spit, sigh, or clatter utensils. The senior adept eats slowly. During the serving of the meal, each action is performed three times, proceeding in order of rank. When the meal concludes, utensils are collected from the lowest to the highest. Food is only received at the proper time; no one may demand it out of turn.

As the meal nears its end, the leftover rice is gathered to be scattered for all beings. No one except the kitchen supervisor may take anything from the kitchen. If the meal is insufficient, one must descend from the hall to eat. Forcing entry into the refectory is called "offending the meal." If one finishes eating before the assembly and rises abruptly, it is called "startling the seat and offending the assembly."

For an ordinary retreat without a master’s offering, the invocation for the master is omitted. After the invocation for all beings concludes, water is passed once more, and utensils are collected. A single strike of the chime signals: *"The meal has concluded. Each may retire to rest as they finish."* The senior adept then collects the utensils in order and returns to their quarters to wash and rinse. The kitchen supervisor ensures no one remains behind. If any rule is violated, the offender is penalized with twenty prostrations before the assembly.

For meals offered by laypeople, the procedures for sitting, rising, and receiving food remain the same. However, when receiving fruit, one must accept it all without selection, lest the layperson feel criticized. After the meal, any remaining fruit is placed before the assembly. A single strike of the chime signals the announcement: *"The assembly’s leftover food is offered for the host’s unrestricted use."* Those who agree remain silent; those who disagree may speak three times. After the announcement, if the retreat official has parents, a master, or elderly or ill individuals, they may take the remaining fruit, rising to announce: *"So-and-so has parents, a master, or elderly or ill individuals at home and wishes to take this fruit to offer to the senior adept."* The senior adept replies: *"Take it with blessing."* If no such need exists, the host collects the fruit for their own unrestricted use.

Someone may ask: *"Should rice be set aside for all beings during the meal?"*
The answer: *"In the world of abandoned worthies, at the beginning of the Primordial Origin, the Perfected of the Dao were commanded to propagate the teachings. They tasted the hundred herbs and discovered the five grains to sustain humanity. All hungry ghosts lack the merit of cultivation. During the meal, seven grains of rice are set aside to repay the virtue of the Perfected and offer them to all beings. This is called 'Shennong’s rice' or 'the rice of all beings.' A single grain can satiate a being for a thousand days."*

Three invocations are recited for the offering of this rice:
First:
*"Today is an auspicious day,
We fast and face the Most High.
At the midday meal from the celestial kitchen,
The host’s blessings are boundless."*

Second:
*"The Two Mysteries ascend the Dao,
The Four Poles are pure and numinous.
The Supreme Unity, the Lord Emperor,
And the hundred spirits are at peace.
They receive sustenance in the Three Palaces,
Irrigating the spirit of the spleen.
They ascend to feast on the Great Harmony,
Partaking of the five fragrances.
The life of the soul and embryo,
The seven fluids flow and settle.
The hundred passages are harmonized,
The five viscera bloom with radiance.
The Twin Sovereigns merge with the light,
Soaring to ascend the Upper Clarity."*

Third:
*"Diligently cultivate the great Dao’s law,
With a refined heart, move the Supreme Darkness.
The Perfected descend, demons and specters retreat,
All malevolent spirits vanish in form.
Seven generations of ancestors are born in the Southern Palace,
The self ascends to the Upper Clarity.
Blessings are universally bestowed,
This food is offered to all beings."*

Two invocations are recited for congee:
First:
*"Today is pure,
Partaking of the immortals’ sustenance.
Incense is offered, congee is eaten,
Lifespan is extended."*

Second:
*"The Qian trigram is firm and beneficial,
The body enters the Dharma King.
Congee enters the belly,
The five viscera are cooled."*

The invocation for rinsing the mouth:
*"Rinsing with willow twigs,
The eyes and vision become clear.
May I obtain one branch
To offer to the Great Dao.
The Dao resides in one tree,
With many leaves and much fragrance.
May I obtain one branch
To offer to the ten directions."*

*The Sutra of the Ascending Mystery* states: *"After midday, one must not eat food containing the qi of grains."*

Those containing the *qi* of grains may not be consumed with near-mouth water, jade fat stone, pine wood, Solomon’s seal, cloud essence, *lingfei* powder, or wolfberry. These medicinal foods may be taken at any time and are not subject to prohibition.

*The Sutra of the Essential Teachings* states: *"At dawn, one eats congee; at midday, one meal. Thereafter, water must not pass the teeth."*

*The Regulations of the Thousand Perfected* states: *"When partaking in a fast, five matters must be observed: first, one should pace according to the shadow of the sundial; second, strike the bell to assemble the assembly; third, perform ablutions with water; fourth, sound the chime before eating; fifth, recite the invocation for the ancestral masters. If these five matters are not performed, the fast is not pure, and the meal is considered stolen."*

The regulations state: *"All who enter the fast must adhere to established rules. If a prior agreement is made for three *dou*, it may not be increased or decreased—decreasing constitutes theft, and taking more is likewise theft. When distributing food, fruits and vegetables and above must not be taken in excess. If one greedily takes more, this also constitutes theft. When distributing food, the senior must consider the needs of the junior and take only what is necessary."*

*The Sutra of the Ascending Mystery* states: *"Superiors and inferiors must regard one another with mutual care."*

The regulations state: *"The Dao’s method is impartial, making no distinction between intimacy or estrangement, nor between high or low. Dwelling together in one assembly, protection and care must be without bias. If individuals act out of personal sentiment, granting requests as they arise, this neglects the assembly, leading to its ruin. All should be cautious of this."*

The regulations state: *"If, during a fast, a layperson comes to beg for food, the kitchen supervisor must inquire whether they can observe the fast. If they can, food is given; if not, the consequences of cause and effect are explained, so they may understand sin and blessing. This is not out of stinginess but to guide them toward awakening, strictly upholding the Dao’s method. This is the foremost act of compassion. If no explanation is given, beings incur guilt."*

The regulations state: *"When establishing a Daoist sanctuary, all must partake in the communal meal. When the fast is observed and offerings are made, all directions share equally. The chime is struck and the bell is rung to summon the assembly. Those not observing the fast may not partake in this meal. Together, they cultivate blessings and goodness, with no barrier between Daoists and laypersons. To restrict guests or close the door is a shared disgrace."*

If asked, *"The food is limited, yet guests are unrestricted. Without divine powers, how can we provide for all?"* The answer is: *"Hosts and guests must mutually accommodate. If the original offering is meager and the guests numerous, it is permissible to divide equally, reducing portions to share. This is human nature; one should not refuse outright."*

The regulations state: *"To take communal food for private gifting or to divert it to one’s own quarters is considered theft."*

The regulations state: *"Communal property is shared by all directions, mutually entrusted across a hundred generations. It may not be arbitrarily used at will, nor may one act without collective restraint, diverting it for private use. Such actions incur the five rebellious sins."*

The regulations state: *"To steal communal fasting offerings or, as a leader, appropriate them for oneself is to fall into the Hell of Fire Pits, from which one emerges only after ten thousand kalpas, then enters Fengdu for sixty years of torment, thereafter becoming a hungry ghost for eternity."*

---

**Notes on Dietary Prohibitions and Admonitions**

To secure well-being, one must first rely on food and breath. While food nourishes the five viscera, it may also harm the six bowels. By adhering to prohibitions and admonitions, one may honor life.

*Master Lao’s Scripture on Nurturing Life* states: *"When eating, take care not to consume food that is scalding hot, lest sweat drip into the meal and harm the body. When eating cold food, do not let the teeth ache; when eating hot food, do not scorch the lips. After eating, rub the abdomen three hundred times to dispel the hundred diseases. Mercury must not be ingested, as it severs teeth and hair, causing swelling and tooth loss. Alum must not be taken as medicine, for it ruptures the heart and liver. Cinnamon must not be chewed daily, as it damages bones and tendons. On an empty stomach, one must not encounter foul corpses, for corpse *qi* enters the spleen, causing a white film to form on the tongue and chronic bad breath. Wine may be drunk upon encountering such. Eating hot food while chilled causes piercing wind. Washing the hair after a full meal causes head wind. Covering food with copper vessels allows sweat to drip into the meal, causing malignant sores and internal abscesses. After eating hot food, do not wash the face, lest the complexion fade and one feel as though insects crawl upon it. Do not gulp water hastily, lest it cause *qi* illness and watery accumulations, forming masses. Do not lie down immediately after eating, lest it cause *qi* illness, back pain, and injury. Eating to sudden fullness when hungry reverses the flow, causing heart confusion and food accumulations. Galloping after a full meal causes heart masses over time; drinking water after galloping harms the heart, leading to chronic heart pain; running after a full meal causes *qi* illness over time. Do not habitually drink from mountain springs or flowing streams, lest it cause goiter. After eating, one must move about to circulate the hundred vessels. Salt harms the sinews, sourness harms the bones, bitterness harms the heart—none should be consumed in excess. Great thirst obstructs the flow of *qi*; great fullness prevents *qi* from being stored. Do not eat raw food, lest it harm the intestines and stomach; cold food harms the bladder; excessive eating blocks the hundred vessels. Hot food harms the intestines; cold food harms the stomach. Do not consume excessive sour honey. When passing water, do not drink if your reflection is not visible in the liquid. Peaches, being sour, cause heat when eaten in excess. Apricots, being sour, harm the sinews and bones and shorten lifespan. Pears, being bitter, cause cold in the center and harm those with metal wounds or women in childbirth. Cherries, being sweet, harmonize the center and benefit the spleen’s *qi*. Grapes, being sweet, treat water in the intestines, harmonize the center, and benefit urination. Wine made from them, when consumed over time, unblocks the waterways. Scallions, being pungent, benefit the sick, dispel cold and heat, and when pounded and applied to metal wounds, remove heat and water *qi*. Alfalfa, being sweet, may be consumed over time to benefit people. Smartweed and mustard, being pungent, dispel kidney *qi* and cause cold when consumed over time. Barley, being sour, causes heat when eaten. Elm seeds, being pungent, dispel evil and, when consumed over time, harmonize the five viscera and kill intestinal parasites."*

Wheat, being sweet, dispels heat and removes intestinal heat;
Rice, being sweet, causes excessive heat in the body;
Soybeans make the body heavy and generate excessive heat;
Turnip, also called *luófú*, being bitter, benefits the five viscera, lightens the body, and augments *qi*.

---

**The Ninth Chapter of the *Jiujing* of the Daoist Precepts and Rituals**
**The Tenth Chapter of the *Zhao* of the Daoist Precepts and Rituals**
Compiled by the Three Caverns Daoist Master Zhu Faman

**The First Treatment: The Treatment of the Hall**
The first treatment is the hall. To purify the hall, one must first mend its flaws, then settle its influence upon the human heart below and align it with the stars above. Each constructed hall must follow its prescribed rituals.

The *Taizhen Ke* states:
*"Establish the Hall of the Celestial Master, with a square foundation of eighty-one paces, following the nine-nine calculation, to elevate the yang *qi*."*

The central hall is named *Chongxu Hall*, with one courtyard, seven frameworks, six rooms, and twelve *zhang* in span. Above the hall’s main structure, in the central two rooms, construct a single-tiered *Chongxuan Platform*.

At the center of the platform, install a great incense burner, five *chi* in height, to perpetually burn incense.

Open three doors—east, west, and south—with windows beside each door. At both ends, construct horse paths. Beneath the southern door and above the eaves, set up a platform for the descendants of the Celestial Master to receive morning rites.

The eight great halls: those Daoists dwelling in mountain asceticism, aiding the world, may ascend the platform to pay homage. All other officials—great or small, inner or outer *sacrificial officers*—gather in the grand hall and pay homage from below.

Twelve *zhang* north of the *Chongxuan Platform*, construct the *Chongxian Hall*, with seven rooms, fourteen *zhang* in span, and seven frameworks. The east side is the *Yangxian Chamber*, the west side the *Yinxian Chamber*.

South of the *Chongxuan Platform*, twelve *zhang* away and near the southern gate, construct a five-room, three-frame gatehouse.

The eastern door of the gatehouse serves as the residence of the *Xuanwei Sacrificial Officer*;
The western room of the gatehouse serves as the residence of the *Chashu Sacrificial Officer* overseeing *qi* inspection.

The remaining small residences—too numerous to list—follow this pattern in the twenty-four halls.

The Precept states:
*"Through prolonged study and accumulated virtue, one receives the mandate to become the Celestial Master, appointing two thousand four hundred male and female sacrificial officers, each leading households and transforming the people. The yin officials are called *luozhi*, the yang officials are called *zaishou*."*

It is further said:
*"Each household must establish a *Jingchongyang* altar, offering five *dou* of rice to nurture the transformative forces and harmonize the five *xing* energies. The household register is inscribed upon the rice, renewed annually according to the assembly."*

On the first day of the tenth month, gather at the Celestial Master’s Hall to deliver the rice to the *Tiancang* (Celestial Granary) and the fifty-*li* watchtowers, to prevent famine and the hardships of travelers in years of scarcity. Travelers need not bring provisions.

It is further said:
*"On the first day of the tenth month, those who gather to deliver rice to the Celestial Master’s Hall do so because, at the beginning of the tenth month, the primordial yang *qi* of the Great Sun begins to generate, thus commanding humans to receive the mandate of life."*

On the fifth day of the tenth month, at dawn, assemble at the Celestial Master’s Hall. All must line up before the hall, facing north and kneeling in submission, as the hall’s overseer proclaims the precepts and prohibitions. No private carrying of impure food is permitted, nor defiling the hall’s purity at its gates.

The fifth day of the tenth month is for registering births.

On the seventh day of the seventh month, the *Zhonghui* assembly is held to register life records and assess the achievements of inner and outer Daoists in harmonizing the ritual *qi*.

On the seventh day of the first month, officials are promoted in the *Cihui* assembly, and rewards are bestowed for merit and advancement.

It is further said:
*"Those who enter the hall with laziness, neglecting filial piety and failing to heed the overseer’s instruction, shall be admonished;*
*Those who arrive at the hall with improper conduct, speaking without restraint, or violating orders, shall be punished with the four penalties."*

**The Second Treatment: The Second Treatment**

**The Treatments of Dao/Way**

The *ke* (precepts) states:
The *Shan* (Mountain) Treatment, the *Zhongmào* (Bell) Treatment, the *Báishí* (White Stone) Treatment, and the *Gānghù* (Gang Mutual) Treatment—these four are called *pǐn* (grades), and are known as *bièzhì* (Distinct Treatments) when practiced externally, and *bèizhì* (Comprehensive Treatments) when practiced internally, totaling twenty-four. However, the Comprehensive Treatments are twenty-eight in number.

The Distinct Treatments differ from the *Sān Bā* (Three Eights) Treatments: the *Jíyáng* (auspicious yang) Treatment, the *Píngdū* (leveling) Treatment, the *Āféng* (encountering) Treatment, the *Címǔ* (benevolent mother) Treatment, the *Huángjīn* (yellow gold) Treatment, the *Tàihuá* (great transformation) Treatment, the *Qīngchéng* (green city) Treatment, and the *Éméi* (elegant allure) Treatment—these eight are called *yóuzhì* (wandering treatments);

The *Dàiyuán* (substitute primal) Treatment, the *Zūnlǐng* (gourd leader) Treatment, the *Làixiāng* (lazy village) Treatment, the *Gōngmù* (revered mother) Treatment, the *Pínggōng* (leveling reverence) Treatment, the *Kēlǐ* (preceptal) Treatment, the *Jièruǎn* (borrowed oblation) Treatment—these eight are called *pèizhì* (paired treatments).

The *Zhèngzhì* (Rectified Treatments) number twenty-four:
The *Běimáng* (northern tomb) Treatment, the *Yùjú* (jade district) Treatment, the *Zhǔbù* (master register) Treatment, the *Pínggāng* (leveling ridge) Treatment, the *Gōngmù* (revered mother) Treatment, the *Hòuchéng* (rear city) Treatment, the *Jiǎnkǒu* (junction mouth) Treatment, and the *Yúntái* (cloud platform) Treatment—these eight are the lower-grade *zhì* (treatments);

The *Pínggài* (leveling canopy) Treatment, the *Méngqín* (mysterious Qin) Treatment, the *Běnzhú* (root bamboo) Treatment, the *Chóujīng* (dense glutinous rice) Treatment, the *Běipíng* (northern leveling) Treatment, the *Yǒngquán* (gushing spring) Treatment, the *Lìshàng* (rite above) Treatment, and the *Chānglì* (prosperous benefit) Treatment—these eight are the middle-grade *zhì* (treatments);

The *Zhēnduō* (true abundance) Treatment, the *Qínzhōng* (Qin central) Treatment, the *Gēngchú* (replacement elimination) Treatment, the *Gězuǒ* (hemp rags) Treatment, the *Líyuán* (flowing Yuan) Treatment, the *Hèmíng* (crane's cry) Treatment, the *Lùtáng* (deer hall) Treatment, and the *Yángpíng* (yang leveling) Treatment—these eight are the upper-grade *zhì* (treatments).

These forty-four external *zhì* (treatments) are received with the offering of a single garment, straw sandals, paper, ink, brushes, and a book-knife.

The *Xīngsù* (Star Host) Treatment, numbering twenty-eight, is also called the *Shàngzhì* (Upper Treatment), the *Dàzhì* (Great Treatment), and the *Zhèngzhì* (Rectified Treatment). Established on the seventh day of the seventh month in the first year of *Shànghuáng* (Upper Emperor), by the *Tàishàng Dàdào Jūn* (Great Dao/Way Lord Above), and transmitted by *Dàodòng* (Dao/Way Ridge) for propagation.

The Three Treatments' jurisdictions:
The Three Treatments' jurisdictions are as follows: the *Yángpíng* (yang leveling), *Lùtáng* (deer hall), and *Hèmíng* (crane’s cry) Treatments, which oversee those born under the *chén* (hour) of *shēng* (birth).

The *Lìyuán* (Lì Element) and *Gěguī* (Gě’s Splendor) Treatments—these two oversee those born in the *mǎo* (hour).
The *Gēngchú* (Renewal Removal) and *Qínzhōng* (Qin Central) Treatments oversee those born in the *yín* (hour).
The *Zhēnduō* (True Abundance) and *Chānglì* (Flourishing Profit) Treatments, subordinate to the three upper Treatments, oversee those born in the *chǒu* (hour).
The *Yǒngquán* (Gushing Spring) and *Běimáng* (Northern Tomb) Treatments oversee those born in the *zǐ* (hour).
The *Chóujīng* (Dense Rice) and *Běnzhú* (Original Bamboo) Treatments oversee those born in the *hài* (hour).
The *Méngqín* (Mysterious Qin) and *Pínggài* (Leveling Cover) Treatments oversee those born in the *xū* (hour).

The next eight Treatments:
The *Xuánlǎo* (Mysterious Elder) and *Yúntái* (Cloud Platform) Treatments oversee those born in the *xū* (hour);
The *Jìkǒu* (Mouth of the River) and *Hòuchéng* (Rear City) Treatments oversee those born in the *yǒu* (hour);
The *Gōngmù* (Public Admiration) and *Pínggāng* (Level Ridge) Treatments oversee those born in the *shēn* (hour);
The *Zhǔbù* (Chief Clerk) and *Yùjú* (Jade Bureau) Treatments—this one Treatment is not of the Mountain Remnant, but of the Northern Tomb—three Treatments oversee those born in the *wèi* (hour).

The lower eight Treatments:
The *Tàishàng* (Supreme) Treatment was established by the *Tàishàng* (Supreme) on the seventh day of the first month, *Hàn’ān* era’s second year, at noon, when the twenty-four Treatments were entrusted to the Celestial Master.
The *Gānghù* (Ridge Mutual) and *Báishí* (White Stone) Treatments oversee those born in the *wǔ* (hour);
The *Zhōngmào* (Bell Prosperity) and *Jùshān* (Complete Mountain) Treatments oversee those born in the *wèi* (hour).

**The Four Treatments’ Chambers**
The Four *Jìngshì* (Serene Chambers) are as follows:
The *Kē* (Statutes) state:
*"When Dao/Way citizens enter transformation, each household establishes a Serene Chamber, facing west toward east, with a single incense burner placed beneath the western wall. The Celestial Master, as the sovereign of the Dao/Way Treatments, enters the Serene Chamber: first facing west, he reveres the incense burner for the Master, bows three times, and offers three incense. He then turns north, then east, then south. Upon completion, he exits without turning back."*

The *Kē* (Statutes) state:
*"At the mid-morning hour, entering the minor Treatment, first face west and bow to the Celestial Master, then turn east and bow to the Supreme."*

Upon exiting, he reviews the *lù* (scrolls), facing east to present petitions.

The *Kē* (Statutes) state:
*"At the mid-morning hour of worship, first face north and bow to the Supreme, then face east and bow to the Nine-Qi Venerable, then face south and bow to the Dao/Way Virtue Lord, then face west and bow to the Celestial Master. The minor Treatments follow the same."*

The *Xuándū Lǜ* (Mysterious Capital Statutes) states:
*"For households that establish Serene Chambers in accordance with Heaven’s Virtue, on days *jiǎ*, *yǐ*, *bǐng*, and *dīng*, the earth is prepared for entering the Serene Chamber.*

*The minor Treatment measures eight *chǐ* in width and ten *chǐ* in length;
The middle Treatment measures twelve *chǐ* in width and fourteen *chǐ* in length."*

*"The Great Treatment measures sixteen *chǐ* in width and eighteen *chǐ* in length;
The altar and incense burner are placed at the center of the chamber.
Violating the Treatment incurs a penalty of one *jì* (cycle of years) in the *suàn* (reckoning of fate).

It is further stated: Households are called *jìng* (Serene Chambers), while Master’s residences are called *zhì* (Treatments).

The Statutes decree:
On days *bǐng-yín*, *dīng-mào*, *bǐng-shēn*, *dīng-yǒu*, *bǐng-xū*, and *jiǎ-xū*, one must not enter the Treatment.
Violating this statute incurs a penalty of forty-five *suàn* (reckonings of fate).

When entering the Treatment, one must sit upright facing east, harmonizing the Five-Qi (directions), first invoking the *Gōngcāo* (Functional Officers) and *yīnyáng* (yin-yang officials), then bowing reverently with a focused mind and proper posture.

The *Tàizhēn Kē* (Statutes of Supreme Authenticity) states:
For laypersons receiving the Treatment or standing Treatment, their halls and lodgings must be orderly and clean, with clear distinctions for entry and exit, located in the southwest of their residences, and dedicated to scriptural offerings.
The Statutes further decree:
‘The incense and lamps must be kept burning continuously, the practitioner must cultivate the spirit and master the Authentic Dao, so that the Authentic Spirit may descend.
One must not receive the Treatment in an empty capacity, without proper lodging, while pursuing worldly gain, or through begging and petitioning. If impure and chaotic, it offends the spirits, causing confusion among humans and divine beings, and invites ridicule and transgression.
Instead, one must reside in modest dwellings, wear coarse clothing, eat simple food, and earnestly prepare the Treatment hall.’

If there is confusion or error in the rites, the Five Punishments shall be applied.
The *Tàizhēn Kē* further states:
‘When children receive the *Yījiāngjūn* (One-General) scroll and the nine ranks of *shùgēnglìng* (Office and Soldiery), this is known as the *Tīancháo* (Heavenly Register). They may then follow and enter the Serene Chamber or Treatment, bowing three times toward the *shěng* (Provincial Office), and pleading for urgent mercy.’

The Statutes decree:
‘Those receiving ten *Jiāngjūn* (Generals) or more must also deploy the Office and Soldiery, bowing three times at the mid-morning hour, and petitioning for urgent matters.’

The Statutes further state:
‘First, the *Gùnjīn* (Replacement) must strictly adhere to the ancient rites, diligently recite the scriptures, absorb the *Zhēnqì* (Authentic Qi), and comply with commands to act. If they fail, their descendants will suffer many calamities, and their parents may receive it in their stead. Normally, one must be seven years old to receive this office.
Second, the *Jìmèng* (Register of Merit) must strictly follow the *Lǚzhēn* (Lü’s Authenticity), reverently uphold the precedents. If they violate them, they will be punished with illness and misfortune, or even lose what they have received. If they repent and seek forgiveness, they may receive the later edicts, be instructed earnestly, abandon vice for virtue—this is not restricted by year.
Third, the *Tóngzǐ* (Child-Soldier) receives the *Yījiāngjūn* scroll. Boys and girls from eight to nineteen years old are all considered *tóngzǐ*, and their movements must be guided by gradual enlightenment, nurturing the *Xuānfēng* (Mysterious Winds).
Fourth, the *Chéngrén* (Adult) receives the *Shíjiāngjūn* (Ten-General) scroll. Men and women at twenty years old are considered adults. Those who are infirm, aged, or feeble-minded, unable to endure transformation, must be examined according to their capacity and gently guided toward enlightenment.
Fifth, the *Qīshíwǔjiāngjūn* (Seventy-Five-General) scroll contains seventy-five *jiāngjūn* (Generals), some bearing Daoist and some bearing spiritual essence—*yīnqì* (yin-qi) as Daoist, *yángqì* (yang-qi) as spiritual. Spiritual essence governs the earth, Daoist essence governs the heavens; the heavens are associated with *wén* (literature), the earth with *wǔ* (martial); martial governs the interior, literary the exterior. If one attains this rank within the Master’s gate, they must bow in submission to the left and right, first receiving the spiritual essence. If one is outside the Master’s gate, coming and going for consultation, they must first receive the Daoist essence. After receiving the Daoist essence, they advance to receive the spiritual; after receiving the spiritual, they advance to receive the Daoist. The total reaches one hundred and fifty, completing the scrolls. The sixth, the *Bǎiwǔshíjiāngjūn* (One-Hundred-Fifty-General) scroll, is also under the same Master, requiring no repeated oath-taking. If the previous Master granted seventy-five, and another Master later grants seventy-five more, one must provide firewood, rice, paper, ink, brushes, writing knives, vermilion, and silk as prescribed. If one has not yet received the One-General, Ten-General, or Seventy-Five-General scrolls but possesses high virtue, they may receive the One-Hundred-Fifty-General scroll directly.

It is further stated: Though the sequence of receiving spiritual essence before Daoist essence follows the principle of continuity, the proximity to Heaven’s Dao dictates the order—near or far—using either a *yīyù* (one talisman) or a *yīsù* (one seal), without regard to whether one is inside or outside the Master’s gate.

The above six stipulations are known as the *Wàiquè* (External Registers).
In general, the progression from the lowest to the highest rank follows the previous examples. If a girl-child attains the merit of an adult, or if an adult has not yet received their rank but displays extraordinary virtue and deeds, they may all surpass and receive the One-Hundred-Fifty-General scroll, first the Daoist essence, then the spiritual, transforming from external to internal, all using the *Hēiguān* (Black Official) seal.

If one first attains the rank under one Master, receiving the One-General, Ten-General, or Seventy-Five-General scrolls, and later reaches the One-Hundred-Fifty-General rank under another Master, they must all be recorded, and the scrolls must be carried and worn, with the later ones placed behind the earlier ones, all sealed in boxes. One must not lose them, as losing them incurs a penalty of half the reckoning of fate, and they need not be received again. Those who have received both the Ten-General and Seventy-Five-General scrolls, if later receiving from another Master, must surrender their previous scrolls to the new Master, and the accumulated merit shall be transferred.

The descendants of the *Dòngyuān Tiānshī* (Cavernous Abyss Celestial Master) must receive the *qìquè* (Qi-Register) scrolls but not the Treatment scrolls. The Treatment officials are all subordinate to the *Huángtán* (Imperial Altar), and their ranks must be fully prepared or not at all.

After receiving the scrolls, the priority is to perform morning worship, followed by petitioning for mercy and saving lives, invoking the Mysterious Spirits, and addressing them as follows: ‘[Name], a General-scroll male/female of the [rank], does not presume to call oneself a subject.’
When using talismans to command water, heal illnesses, or avert calamities, if one demonstrates diligent merit, the Master shall promote them accordingly.

The *Míngqìquè* (Illuminated Registers) include:
- The *Huángshū Qì* (Yellow Book Register), *Liùjiǎ Qì* (Six Jia Register), *Shénquè* (Spiritual Scroll), *Zhēnquè* (Authentic Scroll), and *Shèngxiānquè* (Saintly Daoist Scroll)—these two registers and three scrolls illuminate the *Tǔdé* (Earth Virtue), with faith as the foremost principle, governing the *Zhǔfǔ* (Master’s Office) monthly, and aligning with the *Huángdào Xiān* (Yellow Daoist Path), subduing the *Zhìxīng* (Zhi-star).
- The *Chìjiè Qì* (Red Boundary Register), *Qīngtóng Qì* (Green Child Register), *Chìjiǎ* (Red Jia), and *Qīngjiǎquè* (Green Jia Scroll)—these two registers and two scrolls illuminate the *Huǒdé* (Fire Virtue), with ritual precedence, governing the day, and aligning with the *Chìdào Xiān* (Red Daoist Path), subduing the *Yínghuòxīng* (Yinghuo-star).
- The *Zhēnyī Qì* (Authentic Unity Register), *Sānyǒuqì* (Three-Five Register), *Xuánquè* (Mysterious Scroll), and *Yuánmìngquè* (Primordial Command Scroll)—these two registers and two scrolls illuminate the *Mùdé* (Wood Virtue), with benevolence as the foundation, governing the *Suìxīng* (Sui-star).
- The *Sānyī Qì* (Three-One Register) and *Wúzhōngquè* (Limitless Term Scroll)—this one register and one scroll illuminate the *Shuǐdé* (Water Virtue), with wisdom as the source, governing the *Chénxīng* (Chen-star).
- The *Wǔsèyī Qì* (Five-Color Unity Register) and *Tàijiǎquè* (Great Jia Scroll)—this one register and one scroll illuminate the *Jīndé* (Metal Virtue), with righteousness as the root, governing the *Tàibáixīng* (Tai Bai-star).

The *Tàiyī Quàn* (Great Unity Talisman) is also known as the *Jīnquàn* (Golden Talisman), for the talisman is another name for the register. The eight registers are fixed in rank, hence the term ‘Great Unity’ encompasses all eight stages.
