Inside, use **xiōng wú (芎藭)、dāng guī (当归)、bái zhì (白芷)、xīn yí (新夷)、dú héng (杜衡)、shān jìng (山蓟)、gé běn (革本)、mùlán ròu (木兰肉)、tú róng (徒蓉)、bó shí (栢实)、cāng yǐ (苍苡)、mí wú (蘼芜)、yǒu dōng huā (欵冬花)、bái héng (白衡)、qín jiāo (秦椒)、shǔ jiāo (蜀椒)、guì (桂)、gān jiāng (乾姜)、fēi lián (飞廉)、fáng fēng (防风)、rén shēn (人参)、jú gěng (桔梗)、bái wēi (白薇)、jīng shí (荆实)**—
a total of **twenty-four substances (凡二十四物)**,
to correspond with the **twenty-four breaths of the year (二十四气)**.
Additionally, **eight toxic medicines (八毒药)** are added,
to correspond with the **eight winds (八风)**:
**wū tóu (乌头)、fù zǐ (附子)、lí lú zào (梨芦皂)、jiá wǎng cǎo (荚罔草)、fán shí (矾石)、bàn xià (半夏)、huá yīn (华阴)、xì xīn (细辛)**—
bringing the total to **thirty-two substances (都合三十二物)**.
All are **ground into powder (□咀)**,
with the **toxic medicines (毒药)** placed beneath the **fragrant medicines (香药)**,
and the **pillow is filled in sequence (按次满枕中)**,
then enclosed in a **cloth pouch (为布囊)**.

If one **wraps the pillow with clothing (以衣枕之)** for **one hundred days (百日)**,
the **sinews and bones will become strong (筋骨劲强)**,
and the **face will radiate luster (面有光泽)**;
for **one year (一年)**,
the **entire body will be fragrant (身尽香)**.

「Four years’ use will turn white hair black,
and cause new teeth to grow.

Normally, one should prepare a separate **cloth pouch (帏囊)**,
and when lying down, immediately rise to cover oneself with it.
Do not let the **qi (气)** escape,
and each year, renew the medicine,
totaling **thirty-two substances (三十二种)**.
For each substance, the amount is **one dou and two sheng (一斗二升)**,
then crushed into **three he (三合)** of powder,
or **seven jiu (七圭)** and **eight cuo (八撮)**—
which is **four parts of a cuo (五分撮之四)**.

Moreover, since the **nature of medicines varies (药体有虚实)**,
one should also weigh them proportionately.

According to the *Dà Sàn Jiā Pǐn Yào* (大散家品药),
the following are the traditional **dosages (旧率)**:
**Róng (蓉)**, ten **liang (两)** and sixteen **zhu (铢)**, yields one **sheng (升)** and four **he (合)**;
**Gān jiāng (乾姜)**, three **liang** and twenty-two **zhu**, yields five **he**;
**Guì (桂)**, three **liang** and fourteen **zhu**, yields five **he**;
**Fáng fēng (防风)**, two **liang** and four **zhu**, yields four **he**;
**Jiégēng (桔梗)**, two **liang** and nine **zhu**, yields four **he**;
**Rén shēn (人参)**, one **liang** and twenty **zhu**, yields three **he**;
**Huáng qín (黄苓)**, seventeen **zhu**, yields one **he**;
**Xì xīn (细辛)**, one **liang** and twenty-one **zhu**, yields three **he**;
**Fù zǐ (附子)**, nineteen **zhu**, yields one **he**;
**Xiá líng (狭苓)**, eighteen **zhu**, yields one **he**;
**Fán shí (矾石)**, one **liang** and six **zhu**, yields one **he**;
**Mǔ lì (牡砺)**, sixteen **zhu**, yields one **he**.

These are the **standard measurements (准量)** for the respective medicines.
For the **White Tiger (白虎)** days within each **ten-day cycle (旬)**,
use the medicine that **counteracts the corresponding cycle (所克本旬)**:
The **White Tiger** day in the **Jiāzǐ (甲子)** cycle falls on **Chén (辰)**,
in the **Jiāxū (甲戌)** cycle on **Mǎo (卯)**,
in the **Jiāshēn (甲申)** cycle on **Wǔ (午)**,
in the **Jiāwǔ (甲午)** cycle on **Zǐ (子)**,
in the **Jiāchén (甲辰)** cycle on **Yín (寅)**,
and in the **Jiāyín (甲寅)** cycle on **Yǒu (酉)**.

When entering the mountains to **ward off tigers (辟虎)**,
this must also be done according to the **fixed precepts (定诀)**.

One must **strictly observe the rules of weighing and preparation (秤仪戒律)**,
as detailed in the **Fourteenth Chapter of the *Mìaojùan* (妙卷)**.」

The **Three Cavern Daoist** Zhu Faman compiled the **Daoist Rites for Life and Death**, with a preface.

Faman, recognizing my youthful ignorance,
could not fully grasp the **pulse regulations (脉制)**,
and thus harbored deep doubts.

Upon reflection, I found **Master Da Meng Jingyi (大孟景翼)**,
whose courtesy name was **Fumu (辅明)**,
who, when questioned by **Prince Wenhui of Qi (齐文惠太子)** on the Daoist rites for the dying,
quoted the *Lülián Jīng (法轮经)*:
*"The teacher is like a father.
If I had no teacher, I could not see the scriptures;
now that I see the teachings, I know the Dao."*

The *Huángluàn Jiǎnwén (黄箓简文)* records the **precepts for honoring teachers (事师之科典)**.
Thus, in studying the Dao and cultivating the Way,
one must keep the teacher before them.
Having received their **initiation (开度)** and **transmission (禀佩经图)**,
they rescue the three worlds and safeguard the living and the dead.

The scripture states:
*"He who gazes upon the sacred texts
shall join the Nine Ancestors as an Immortal."*

Thus, the **True Persons** say:
*"If I had no teacher, I could not learn the Dao nor attain it."*

Those who manifest in this world in bodily form
all reveal the **trace of the teacher-disciple lineage (师资之迹)**.
Some, when departing, leave behind a **sword or staff (剑杖)** as a sign—
this is but a **transference of form (托物)**,
to show that **transformation is ever-changing (变化无常)**.

Only those who embody the Dao and penetrate the **True (冥真)**
naturally transcend the **fear of death and love of life (欣生恶死)**.

For **human nature** inherently bears **filial piety (孝性)**,
moving even the heavens and earth,
reaching the divine and the numinous.
The sorrow of loss arises spontaneously at every turn—
though wise and foolish differ in degree,
their love and aversion remain unchanged.
Thus, the **Daoist and the secular** diverge,
and the **Great Way** remains unaltered.

In ancient times, **Confucius**, as the **Master of Ruism (儒学之师)**,
only taught the **rites of this world (世间典礼)**—
burial beneath the **sacrificial mound (洒水之上)**,
with disciples in **three years of mourning (服三年)**,
and **Zigong dwelling in a hut by his grave for six years (庐住冢边六载)**.
A hundred families followed him in residence,
earning the name **"People of Kong’s Village (孔里之民)"**,
with sacrifices offered in all four seasons without ceasing.

How much more so in the **Daoist tradition (道家法门)**,
where the **master-artisan (宗匠)** bestows **virtue and splendor (德瑜)** upon the **Three Realms (三界)**,
and **conduct transcends the Six Paths (六道)**—
furthermore, uniting with the **Ultimate Truth (至真)**,
merging with the **Eternal Stillness (常寂)**.
This is where the **inner and outer realms diverge (内外悬殊)**,
beyond the grasp of mere **emotion and calculation (情数)**.
Thus, I establish these **rites (立仪)**,
to exhaustively outline them.

Moreover, **Little Meng (小孟)** refined the earlier rites,
adding what was most essential.
**Stone Well Master (石井公)** composed his work,
citing the *Bàopǔ Nèipiān (抱朴内篇)*:
*"In the Han dynasty, Empress Dowager followed Master Xiahou Sheng in studying the *Shàngshū (尚书)*,
and granted him **one hundred jin of gold** and countless other gifts.
Upon his death, she bestowed **two million coins** on his family
and wore **white mourning for five days (素服五日)**.

Emperor Cheng, while still in the **Eastern Palace (东官)**,
received the *Lúnyǔ (论语)* from Zhang Yu,
and immediately **elevated his status**,
granting him the title **Marquis of Guannei (关内侯)**,
with **one thousand households in fief (食邑千户)**,
and the post of **Grand Officer of Light (光禄大夫)**,
along with **one thousand jin of gold**.
Later, he was promoted to **Chancellor (丞相)** and ennobled as **Marquis of Anchang (安昌)**.
When Yu grew old, he was granted a **luxurious carriage with four horses (安车驷马)**,
**one hundred jin of gold**, and **one million coins**.
When Yu fell ill, the heir apparent **personally knelt at his bedside (亲拜禹床下)**.

Emperor Zhang, while still in the **Eastern Palace (东宫)**,
received the *Xiàojīng (孝经)* from Huan Rong.
Upon ascending the throne, he appointed Rong as **Grand Master of Sacrifices (太宰)**.
The emperor **visited Rong’s home**, seating himself facing east to converse,
and held a banquet for **hundreds of officials and Rong’s disciples**.
The emperor **personally held the scripture and expounded its meaning (持业赞说)**,
granting Rong the title **Marquis of Guannei (关内侯)**,
with **five thousand households in fief (食邑五千户)**.
When Rong fell ill, the emperor **visited his home**,
entering the lane **on foot (下车把卷)**,
hurrying as if paying **disciples’ respects (趋如弟子之礼)**.
Upon Rong’s death, the emperor **mourned in white (素服举哀)**."*

All these were for **teaching a single scripture in Ruism (儒学指教一经)**,
yet the **reverence for the teacher was so profound (崇师殷勤)**—
how much more so for those seeking the **True Essence (求真要)**,
who do not follow the **scriptural rites (经诰)**?

Thus, I establish these **rites and precepts (立典仪)**,
instructing the **disciples of the lineage (门中子弟)**,
as compiled by **Master Zhang Xu (张续先生)**—
though some rites differ, some align,
yet these **four sets of rites (四仪)** lack uniformity,
causing later generations to **follow conflicting interpretations (遵奉随执见乖)**.

Now, I **harmonize the four traditions (会四家)**,
condensing them into one volume,
with my **humble annotations (率愚注诀)**,
balancing **differences and similarities (并酌异同)**,
adapting to the **fluctuations of the age (随世沉浮)**,
and adjusting with the **times (逐时增减)**.
Moreover, when the teacher is present,
one must **keep a respectful distance from the table (远离几筵)**,
inquire about their **comfort and health (咨问寒温)**,
and **submit petitions (凭启疏)**.
For fear that **later learners may misjudge priorities (不识重轻)**,
I now record what I have observed,
titling it the *Daoist Rites for Auspicious and Inauspicious Occasions (道士吉凶仪)*,
encompassing **ten examples (合十例)**,
as detailed below:

**First: The General Announcement Rite (通启仪)**
**Second: The Condolence and Funeral Rite (吊丧仪)**

**Third: The Sickness and Illness Rite (疾病仪)**
**Fourth: The Initial Burial Rite (初死小殓仪)**
**Fifth: The Coffin-Entry and Final Burial Rite (入棺大殓仪)**
**Sixth: The Mourning Attire Rite (成服仪)**
**Seventh: The Burial and Escort Rite (葬送仪)**
**Eighth: The Soul-Settling Rite (安灵仪)**
**Ninth: The Disciple’s Return and Observance Rite (弟子还观仪)**
**Tenth: The Soul-Dismissal Rite (除灵仪)**

**Seasonal Temperature Guide (节气温度表):**
First month (正月, **Meng Chun**—Meng Spring), still cold, lingering cold.
Second month (二月, **Zhong Chun**—Zhong Spring), gradually warm, now warm.
Third month (三月, **Ji Chun**—Ji Spring), warm and mild, extremely warm.
Fourth month (四月, **Meng Xia**—Meng Summer), gradually hot, now hot.
Fifth month (五月, **Zhong Xia**—Zhong Summer), scorching hot, intensely hot.
Sixth month (六月, **Ji Xia**—Ji Summer), peak heat, extreme heat.
Seventh month (七月, **Meng Qiu**—Meng Autumn), still hot, lingering heat.
Eighth month (八月, **Zhong Qiu**—Zhong Autumn), gradually cool, very cool.
Ninth month (九月, **Ji Qiu**—Ji Autumn), gradually cold, now cold.
Tenth month (十月, **Meng Dong**—Meng Winter), light cold, now cold.
Eleventh month (十一月, **Zhong Dong**—Zhong Winter), intensely cold, severe cold.
Twelfth month (十二月, **Ji Dong**—Ji Winter), piercing cold, extreme cold.

As **Pei Ju** states:
*"For all petitions and letters henceforth, the first month shall always be recorded as **Meng Chun, still cold (犹寒)**.
Each subsequent month follows the preceding pattern, with adjustments every fifteenth day, or as circumstances require."*

**General Announcement Rite (通启仪) – Revised Format:**
When a **Magistrate or County Official** first arrives,
the Daoist priest delivers a **General Announcement (通启)**.

**Format:**
*"[Name of Daoist Observatory] Daoist Priest [Surname] respectfully petitions (启)."*

*"Respectfully submitted to the Honorable Magistrate (明使君) and the Honorable Chamberlain (记室明府) and attendants (侍者)."*

*"Daoist Priest [Surname], in deep reverence, humbly acknowledges that the Honorable Magistrate’s (or County Official’s) virtue and governance are vast and illustrious, their benevolent decrees clearly proclaimed. By their grace, they have deigned to oversee this humble domain, bringing joy to all within the Daoist and secular communities alike. Though **Meng Chun remains cold (犹寒)**, I dare not presume to inquire after your esteemed health. This humble priest, afflicted by illness, cannot attend as customary. With deepest regret, I submit this petition without further elaboration. [Surname] respectfully petitions (谨启)."*

*"[Month], [Day], [Name of Daoist Observatory] Daoist Priest [Surname] respectfully petitions (启)."*
*All subsequent petitions follow this model.*

**Rules for Condolence Letters (吊书规则):**
If the letter is written **after the Winter Solstice (正冬)**, and it is the first of the two seasonal nodes (二节), the writer must **express personal grief and inquire after the recipient’s own bereavement**, specifically referencing the loss of parents (二亲不存). Examples:
- If one parent has passed and the other lives: *"On this [seasonal node], my grief deepens (某节远感深)."*
- If the mother has passed and the father lives: *"I mourn the late mother (远思前人)."*
- If both parents have passed: *"I recall the departed with renewed sorrow (惟增怀旧)."*

**Salutations in Letters:**
- *"Ton首 (Dunshou)"* and *"叩头 (Koutou)"* are terms of respect.
  - *"Ton首"* is used when the father is alive.
  - *"叩头"* is used when the father has passed.
- These terms are **omitted** when addressing **five-degree mourning relatives (五服卑亲)** or subordinates.
- In responses to **major mourning (重丧)**, *"Ton首"* may be retained; otherwise, it is excluded.

**Addressing Ancestors in Letters:**
- **Great-grandparents (高祖):** *"High Ancestor Father and Mother (高祖翁婆)"*
- **Great-great-grandparents (曾祖):** *"Once-Removed Ancestor Father and Mother (曾祖翁婆)"*
- **Grandparents (祖父母):** *"Ancestor Father and Mother (祖翁婆)"*

**Letter Headings:**
- For **fathers and mothers:** *"Father and Mother’s [Surname]’s Letter (爷娘几前某言疏)"*
- For **wives:** *"[Surname]’s Wife’s Letter (女某氏言疏)"*

**Seasonal Greetings in Letters:**
*"[Month], [Day], [Surname]’s Letter: Though **Meng Chun lingers cold (犹寒)**, I humbly express renewed grief (伏惟增怀)."*
- If the letter is **not written after the Winter Solstice**, or if both parents are alive, the phrase *"伏惟增怀"* is **omitted**, and *"犹寒"* is placed at the beginning instead.

**Long-Absent Correspondence:**
*"Having been separated for some time, my longing knows no measure. Your teachings of [date] have deeply comforted me. Though I have not received recent instruction, I remain in constant dread of the cold. May I humbly inquire after your well-being and daily routines? I trust your health surpasses the ordinary. On [date], by your grace, I was permitted to serve in attendance, though my absence was regrettable. My longing intensifies daily. I pray you take care of your health."*

If writing a **single-sheet letter (单书)**, the date is placed before *"谨疏"*:
*"[Month], [Day], [Surname] respectfully submits (谨疏)."*

**Letters to a Daoist Master (修受道师书):**
If the master is **not a formal teacher (未得法师)**, the salutation reads:
*"Respected Master [Surname], your humble disciple [Name], in reverence, submits this petition on [date]. Though **Meng Chun deepens my grief (远感)**, I humbly express renewed longing (伏惟增怀). May I inquire after your well-being?"*

If the master is **not a teacher**, simplify to:
*"How do you fare?"* (不用 *"起居"* language)

If the disciple has **recently received a letter from the master**:
*"On [date], I was graced with your instruction, which has deeply comforted me. Though I have not yet paid my respects in person, my longing for you grows. I pray you take care of your health. [Surname] respectfully bows twice (再拜)."*

If the disciple has **not received a letter**:
*"Lately, I have not been graced with your teachings. My thoughts for you are ever-present."*

If the disciple has **received a letter**:
*"On [date], I received your instruction, which has deeply comforted me. Though I have not yet paid my respects, my longing for you intensifies. I pray you take care of your health. [Surname] respectfully bows twice (再拜)."*

**Letters from a Master to a Disciple (师与弟子书):**
*"Disciple [Surname], [Position], respectfully reports."*

*"[Month], [Day], [Surname] reports to you: Though **Meng Chun deepens my grief (远感深)**, I have not seen you in some time. My thoughts for you are constant. I write to ease my longing for you in this cold season. How do you fare? I remain as always. Though I have not yet seen you, my heart is filled with sighs. Take care of yourself, for our time together is fleeting. Your master [Surname] reports."*

---

**Letters to Fellow Students (Brothers and Sisters in Learning) (与同学兄姊书):**
*"To my fellow student [Surname], elder brother/sister in learning,*
*I humbly present this letter.*
*On [Month], [Day], I write: Though spring’s distant longing grows, my heart aches anew. Our separation, though brief, has piled sorrow upon sorrow. I received your inquiry of [date], which deeply comforted me. If no letter came, I would say: ‘Not hearing from you, my heart remains cold with longing, and I know not how you fare.’
*Though I have not yet seen you, my gratitude for your kindness only deepens my yearning. I pray you take care. I humbly present this letter, and bow twice in haste."*

---

**Letters to Fellow Students (Younger Siblings) (与同学弟妹书):**
*"To my fellow student [Surname], younger brother/sister,*
*I humbly report.*
*On [Month], [Day], I report: Though spring’s distant thoughts linger, our parting has long endured, and my sorrow deepens daily. My ailments persist, yet I cannot see you—only sighs remain. I pray you take care.
*This letter is brief; I humbly report."*

---

**Letters to Elders (伯叔父母):**
*"To my elder uncle/aunt and parents,*
*[Surname], your junior, humbly presents this letter.*
*Before you, [Surname]’s daughter speaks:*
*‘[Surname]’s daughter humbly presents this letter.’"*

**Letters to Elders (Brothers and Sisters) (与兄姊书):**
*"To my elder brother/sister,*
*[Surname], your junior, humbly presents this letter before you."*

**Letters to Relatives in Mourning (与同宗绝服尊行书):**
*"To my elder uncle/aunt in mourning,*
*[Surname], your junior, humbly presents this letter before you."*

**Letters to Eldest Brother (与堂兄书):**
*"To my elder brother,*
*[Surname], your junior, humbly presents this letter."*

**Letters to Younger Siblings (与弟妹书):**
*"To my younger brother/sister,*
*[Surname], your elder, humbly presents this letter.*
*If from the same clan but no parents remain:*
*‘Spring’s distant longing runs deep; may our sorrow unite.’*
*If to a cousin:*
*‘My heart aches anew.’"*

**Letters to Uncles/Aunts and Aunts-by-Marriage (舅、舅母及姨):**
*"To my uncle/aunt,*
*[Surname], your junior, humbly presents this letter before you."*

**Letters to Nephews/Nieces (与外甥书):**
*"To my nephew/niece,*
*[Surname] humbly presents this letter."*

**Letters to Young Servants (与小童书):**
*"I humbly present my name and rank.*
*[Surname] reports: ‘Spring’s distant longing runs deep.’*
*Humbly presented. The letter omits the name; [Surname] presents."*

**Letters to Acquaintances (与平怀书):**
*"I present my name and rank.*
*[Surname] reports: ‘Spring’s thoughts linger.’*
*Sent humbly. The letter omits the name; [Surname] presents."*

**Letters to Light Acquaintances (与小轻书):**
*"I present my name and rank.*
*The letter omits the name; [Surname] presents."*

**Letters to Distant/Minimal Acquaintances (与极轻书):**
*"Name and rank.*
*Though no words are written, my thoughts labor for you.*
*Receiving your letter of [date] brings comfort—how does your cold endure?*
*My ailments persist; our meeting has no set time—only sighs remain. Take care, for our days are few.*
*[Surname]’s humble letter."*

---

**Condolence Rituals (吊丧仪第二)**

**Condolence to a Governor or Official (吊刺史县令、诸贵重丧启):**
*"To the [Governor/County Magistrate],*
*[Surname], a humble servant, presents this letter.*
*On [Month], [Day],*
*‘Alas, misfortune strikes without warning! Your honored father/mother has passed, leaving me in grief. If of the third rank or higher, an exalted official:*
*‘Your honored mother has departed, and my sorrow knows no bounds. I mourn your loss with tears, yet the pain is unbearable. I dare not bear this grief alone and humbly present this letter without further words.’"*

**Condolence to a Teacher on Parental Loss (吊师遭父母丧启):**
*"To my honored teacher,*
*[Name], your disciple, presents this letter.*
*On [Month], [Day],*
*‘Alas, misfortune is ever-present! Your revered father/mother has passed, and my sorrow tears at my heart. I cannot bear it. My grief knows no bounds, and my heart shatters—how can I endure this? I have not yet paid my respects, and my sorrow chokes me. I humbly present this letter, though my words are inadequate.’*
*[Name] bows twice."*

**Condolence to a Teacher’s Siblings on Parental Loss (师父母丧，吊师兄弟书):**
*"To the filial [Surname], elder brother/sister in mourning,*
*[Surname], your junior, humbly presents condolences.*
*If the father lives but the mother has passed:*
*‘Your honored mother did not live a full life, abandoned by filial care. My sorrow is profound. I mourn your loss with tears, yet the pain is unbearable—how can I bear it? I have not yet paid my respects, and my sorrow only deepens. I humbly send this letter, overwhelmed with grief.’*
*A Daoist presents condolences."*

**Reporting a Teacher’s Death to Fellow Students (Brothers and Sisters) (师丧，报同学兄姊书):**
*"To my fellow student [Surname], elder brother/sister,*
*[Surname] humbly presents this letter.*
*On [Month], [Day],*
*‘Alas, my misdeeds have brought this grief! Though it does not end my life, our revered teacher has passed. I did not receive divine aid, and on [date], they departed—my cries to heaven and earth pierce my bones. I cannot bear it. My sorrow knows no bounds—how can I endure this? My grief is unbearable.’*
*Humbly presented, though my words are inadequate. [Surname] bows twice."*

**Reporting a Teacher’s Death to Fellow Students (Younger Siblings) (师丧，报同学弟妹书):**
*"To my fellow student [Surname], younger brother/sister,*
*[Surname] humbly reports.*
*On [Month], [Day],*
*‘Alas, my misdeeds have brought this grief! Though it does not end my life, our revered teacher has passed. My sorrow tears at my heart, and I cannot bear it. My grief chokes me—this letter is inadequate.’"*

**Reporting a Fellow Student’s Death to a Teacher (同学丧，报师启):**
*"To my honored teacher,*
*[Name] bows and presents this letter.*
*On [Month], [Day],*
*‘Alas, misfortune strikes without warning! My fellow student [Surname] has passed in youth. My sorrow tears at my heart, and I cannot bear it. My grief knows no bounds—how can I endure this? I have not yet paid my respects, and my sorrow chokes me. I humbly present this letter, though my words are inadequate.’*
*[Name] bows twice."*

---

**Illness Rituals (疾病仪第三)**

*"According to the Daoist Master’s teachings:*
*When a Daoist’s illness grows grave and they near their end, they must first bathe in fragrant water, don their robes as usual, and recite the scriptures they have received. They should hang their amulets at the room’s entrance, prepare offerings, and invite virtuous masters to fast, recite scriptures, and confess all sins committed since birth—none hidden, all revealed—to extinguish guilt and pray for blessings."*

*"Master Zhang Xu says:*
*One may recite the *Sutra for Delivering Beings* one or two hundred times.*

*The Daoist Master says:*
*If one must be interred, they should entrust their scriptures and teachings in written form—composing farewell letters and itemized records, clearly noting names and ranks—before reporting to the Celestial Worthy, concealing nothing.*

*The *Mid-Autumn Jade Register* states:*
*When a master is in sorrow, disciples must all bear grief in their hearts, showing no joy in their comings and goings.*

*The *Jade Register* further says:*
*When a master falls ill, disciples must attend closely at their side, watching their breath as if they were parents.*

*The *Jade Register* again states:*
*When a master faces calamity, disciples must gather all fellow students to establish a fast and pray, thus earning meritorious deeds.*

*The *Thousand True Precepts* decree:*
*Those who have left the secular world differ from the common folk, yet even in death, there are no fixed rites—though some ceremonial observances must be followed.
*During illness, recite scriptures with burning incense, day and night without pause.
*At the hour of death, strike the *Wuchang* (the bell of impermanence).
*For the funeral rites, follow the *Five Refined Corpses* method for preparing the deceased.

*When illness strikes, summon the inner assembly to inquire after the sick, inspect the kitchen staff, and have them serve food to the patient.
*In times of sickness, few recover—when one’s fortune and virtue are exhausted, they may seek to flee east or west in desperation. This is but the panic of one whose prior deeds offer no guarantee, now struck with fear and hastening death. Instead, one should cleanse and air the chambers, allowing virtuous masters to come and go, urging the sick to reflect on impermanence—nothing endures. A death record must be written, for life’s measure is short. The dying should be moved to the *Hall of Transformation*.

*This hall should be built in the northwest corner of the observatory, a three-room structure with five beams, facing west-northwest. Within, a high seat shall bear a statue of *Sheng Hu* (the Ascended One), seated on a lotus as large as a person, left hand raised to point at the Celestial Gate, flanked by two True Officers. When the patient fears their end is near, they shall be moved into the hall and placed behind the statue. A yellow brocade banner, twenty-four feet long, shall be hung, its center tied to the left True Officer’s waist, its head grasped by the patient’s hand. The dying must focus their mind and sincere intent, vowing to follow the Two True Ones through the Celestial Gate to the Golden Threshold, and if their time has not yet come, to establish a field of merit.

*Male officials face the left True One; females face the right.
*Once inside this hall, few return unshaken.
*At the moment of death, they may claim they are not yet at their end—only clinging to life through such schemes. A true sage does not dwell on departure or stay.

*The Precepts decree:*
*When male or female officials grow old and sick, nearing death, all household affairs and possessions must be entrusted to trusted disciples. If the master leaves a will, its instructions must be followed. If no will exists, a detailed record of their worldly goods shall be made and shared with disciples who know them, to be used for meritorious deeds or funeral expenses. If no disciples remain, the observatory’s assembly shall decide. If no assembly exists, the Daoist community shall decide—no funds may be given to secular kin. If parents or a master are named in the will, their commands must be obeyed. If no will exists and someone takes what they wish, they commit a *Five Grievous Offenses*; if a disciple cannot restrain them, the matter must be reported to the authorities.*

**Rites for the First Wrapping (小殓仪第四)**
*At the time of death, if the deceased has not been bathed, they need not be placed on a funeral bed. Instead, lay two layers of mats on the ground, south-facing, unchanged. The deceased should wear their usual hand-clasped robes, headwear, and socks. When moving in or out, the *fu* and *lu* talismans shall hang from the left elbow, the bell and seal from the right. Then, lay the lower blanket and cover them with the upper one.

*Pause, then set an offering of white congee to the east of the corpse. Perform the shoulder sacrifice, then lay two side offerings on the ground—male mourners to the left, female to the right. Unlike common practice, do not place a pearl in the mouth to call the soul.

*The offering prayer reads:*
*'To the Imperial Sage of the Wood Cycle, on the first day of the [month], the disciple [Name] respectfully offers pure libations and vegetarian offerings in sacrifice to the master’s spirit.'*

*After this, weep fully in grief.
*This completes the first wrapping."*

**The Great Burial Rite (Fifth Section: Entering the Coffin)**

When the time for the great burial arrives, no incense-waving or bathing is required. The deceased shall be dressed in robes and headgear as worn during fasting, with sash and belt.

First, place a red lacquered coffin within the outer coffin, along with quicklime, *zǐ* wood, seven-star boards, a *shēng* vessel, a *dān* container, and a rooster-shaped pillow. Four bearers shall carry the shrouded body into the inner coffin, placing the ritual tally (*chuáncè*) on the left, the mirror on the right, and both near the head.

Traditionally, the deceased’s personal scriptures and talismans were placed inside the *lìzǐ* (stomach pouch). Now, these are placed outside the coffin, with the head’s talismans arranged separately—this is also acceptable.

The great burial offering shall then be prepared according to the prescribed rites. Use the deceased’s favorite foods from life: spread a mat to the corpse’s west, placing the offerings of fruit and vegetables upon it.

The prayer reads as follows:
*"To the Celestial Officials of the Five Directions, the Three Heavens, and the Martial Generals of the Five Paths, the aged villagers of the *Gāolǐ*, the Subprefect of the Earthly Regions, the Marshal of *Wǔyí*, the True Officials of the Domain, the River God of the Water Palace, the Overseer of the Soul Gate, the Keeper of the Tomb Gate, the Marshals of Mountains, Rivers, and Marshes, and the Directing Envoys of the Auspicious Talismans—
Today, the Three-Cavern Disciple [Name], born in [State]-[County]-[Village]-[Neighborhood], aged [X], having departed at the [hour] of the [day] of the [month], now returns to [Village]-[Mountain] to establish a brick tomb. In life, this person renounced the secular world to follow the Dao, seeking transcendence. They studied and received the Upper Clarity Three-Cavern Great Scriptures, talismans, diagrams, and edicts, embracing the Return to the Great Yin.

Their winter and summer garments, adorned and used in life—totaling [X] items—along with the cypress coffin and burial rites, are now completed. The talismans have been delivered, and the form is now at rest. The scriptures and treasures are secured, the headgear and belt arranged, and the officials ordered to escort without delay. Let no obstruction arise—neither the *Bǐlú* ghosts of the left and right nor the ghosts of other surnames in the four directions may falsely claim names or seize possessions. All must obey the celestial edicts, as decreed in the *Tàixuān Dūguǐ Fa* and the *Nǚqīng Zhào Shū* statutes. Adjustments may be made as circumstances require."*

The Daoist Sage remarks:
*"For those who can prearrange their scriptures in sacred mountains or pure retreats, that is the highest path. There is no need to bury them with the corpse. Even now, many disciples receive teachings yet fail to fully possess the scriptures, nor can they distinguish their own transmitted texts. Why then must they be buried with the corpse? Why prearrange burial in sacred mountains? The scriptures endure across generations, passed down through talismans and diagrams. When the time comes, they may be retrieved from the tomb as needed. Any personal items inside the corpse need not be bound like the vulgar—no grain pouches, waist cords, or final possessions are required.*

Upon completion, the upper lid is placed according to the prescribed method atop the coffin. Next, the items to be buried are arranged at the coffin’s southern end for veneration.

The officiating Daoists, all in ritual attire, stand at the coffin’s southern end. One chants the Three Treasures’ rites, bowing three times to conclude.

**Completion of the Fifteenth Volume of the Essential Rites and Precepts**
**Completion of the Sixteenth Volume of the Essential Rites and Precepts**
**The Red Precepts of the Three-Cavern Daoists**
**Recitation of the Three Contracts**
**Ritual of Penitence and Offerings"**

The assembly prostrates and hears the Most Venerable High True One, whose boundless compassion pervades the Six Paths, whose profound wisdom dwells in silent darkness, and whose pity extends to the Fourfold Birth. Behold this one, whose emotions are drowned in the river of love, whose spirit is submerged in the ocean of suffering! Though their life and death are determined by the roots of virtue and vice, and their karmic bonds remain unsevered, they face endless cycles of birth and death, their fate sealed.

Even if the flame of wisdom burns eternally, its light cannot illuminate their path; though the sacred vessel sails ceaselessly, they have yet to cross to the other shore. Thus, this body is not truly theirs—today, the departed Certain One (某甲) has encountered the Dao and heard the Dharma, received admonitions to cultivate scriptures, and refined their heart with the phoenix’s seal and their spirit with the dragon’s script. Their emotions have transcended the Ten Stages of Transformation, their wisdom penetrated the Nine Abysses, knowing that the Two Worlds are empty and the Four Great Elements are mere illusions. Since their destiny as a transcendent is not yet fulfilled, they must undergo further transformation. Fearful of lingering karmic burdens in life and death, lest they incur retribution for past transgressions, they rely on the Field of Merit and await the power of virtue.

At this moment, the assembly acts on behalf of Certain One’s family, the Five Degrees of the departed, and the Three Teachers of the spirit’s path. With reverent hearts, they prostrate and recite the rites, honoring the Supreme Way of the Limitless Dao and the Ten-Directional Manifested Sage. They pray for divine power to rescue and deliver the departed, to liberate the destitute spirits, to absolve their sins, and grant them all passage to the Pure Land, recognition in the Celestial Palace, and the title *Yùjīng* (玉京). May they attain the Golden Gate of fruition, merge with the Supreme True Dao, and be free to wander at will.

Indeed, the Saints manifest in countless forms to guide beings; the True Transcendents appear in times of need to leave their traces. Sometimes they take physical likenesses, or leave behind teachings and doctrines, leading ordinary mortals away from suffering and its retribution. Yet, though they endure birth and death, their weary minds and spirits remain entangled. How could they know their past karmic causes or foresee their future destinies? Drifting like lost souls, they cycle endlessly through rebirth, as if the compassion of the Saints could not save them, and they are forever bound to the Field of Punishment.

Today, Certain One, though once blessed with rare talents, studied under a teacher, followed the Dao, and practiced the Dharma, has accumulated virtues and deeds. Yet their karmic debts remain unpaid, and their lingering spirits must be assisted by merit. The assembly, in unison, resolves to cultivate the Great Vehicle heart and perform the deeds of the highest noble ones. For all beings in the Ten Directions—emperors, kings, officials, commoners, those who follow the Dharma, artisans, and scholars of the world—they offer sincere devotion, prostrating to express gratitude for the Thirty-Six Volumes of Marvelous Scriptures, the Golden Tablets and Jade Characters, the Dragon Script and Phoenix Colors, the Nine-Grade True Mantras, and the Five-Directional Orthodox Teachings. They earnestly seek the Mysterious Power to broadly deliver both public and private beings, that all may attain the True Transcendents, be enlightened, and possess the Four Penetrations and Six Perfections. May they expound the Three Vehicles, liberate the Five Paths, and all find freedom in the Dao’s realm.

This world’s lives are fleeting, as the Five Constants are mere illusions and the Four Great Elements are hollow. Like flowing water that never stops or fleeting stone-fire, white steeds cannot be detained, and dark shadows pass swiftly. A thousand years are but the blink of an eye; a hundred years are but a fleeting dream. Even if one enjoys longevity like Páng’ān (彭安), they must still perish in the end. Without ascending the dragon’s path, who can escape? If the Great Destiny is to be fulfilled, who can resist its force?

Now, Certain One has abandoned their body, yet their karmic ties persist. Their earthly affairs remain unresolved, and their radiant visage still lingers in this world. Who will meet them in the afterlife? How can their kin grieve, or travelers mourn, when they depart forever, leaving only a watery farewell? The assembly, therefore, universally extends compassion, reaching all beings in the Ten Directions—sentient beings with consciousness, celestial deities and earthly spirits, wronged kin, and equals alike—as well as those suffering in the mortal realm and tormented souls in hell. All beings, whether born from egg, womb, or transformation, prostrate and submit to the Venerable Daoist Master of the Mysterious Middle and the Sage Assembly of the Way. They pray that with the Six Perfections’ wisdom, the Five-Colored Lights may shine, delivering both visible and invisible beings to eternal bliss. May all evil karmic bonds dissolve, and virtuous deeds flourish, that those who recognize the Fourfold Birth may return to the Three Treasures.

The Threefold Rites conclude, and thus the Three Contracts begin.

The past life has faith, yet human life must perish; the great sage rejoices in the precepts of scripture. The above aligns with the intent of the Two *Mèng* (孟) rites.

The Three Contracts conclude, and thereafter, the *Yíwén* (移文) is read—lest it be recited redundantly as in vulgar customs. When the Three Contracts are finished, one reads the *Yíwén*, without need for the customary repetitions of the vulgar.

The rites conclude, and the *Yíwén* is placed within the previous *Lì* (鬲) vessel. Both inner and outer attendants depart with their bows, and the coffin is sealed—yet the nails must not be driven in immediately. A memorial chapter is then inscribed.

Upon completion of the chapter, the nails are driven, the mourners weep, and the hair is scattered in grief. The *Brǎngwài* (吊慰) rites are performed.

**Memorial Chapter Format:**
The memorial chapter is inscribed as follows: *"The undersigned, a male disciple of the Three-Cavern Daoists from Certain Prefecture, County, and Village, and a member of the Supreme Unity Daoist Order, humbly declares: In accordance with the written records, this male disciple, Certain One, was born into the Dao and received the Orthodox Teachings. Though their seals and talismans were prepared, they could not dwell in seclusion to study and recite, nor embrace unity with a tranquil spirit. Instead, they mingled with the world, stained by dust and defiled by karma, their sins accumulating until their life’s merit was exhausted. At this moment, on the Certain Month, Day, and Hour, they depart from this life, bidding farewell to earth and heaven, and pass into the realm of the soil officials.

Since Certain One possessed scriptures, talismans, ritual implements, robes, and bedding in life, today at this hour, their mortal form is interred, their tomb sealed. Fearful that their lingering spirit may offend the departed, or become a malevolent ghost, a mountain demon, or a false spirit, obstructing the dead and preventing their passage, I humbly present these scriptures, burial goods, and talismans as recorded. I now prostrate and offer this memorial chapter, one copy, to the officials overseeing the burial, the attendants, and the supervising officers. May they witness the proper interment and burial of the corpse, ensuring eternal peace for the departed and tranquility for the tomb’s guardians. May the spirit’s rebirth follow their karmic deeds, and after completion of all rites, the assembled officials return to the Celestial Bureau, where they shall receive their rewards and promotions in accordance with the prescribed ranks. Let none be left in despair."*

「For those bearing resentment or grudges, their lingering spirit may offend the living and the dead alike, and thus I earnestly beg for their pardon and forgiveness, as the Way of the Most High distinguishes.  This is the memorial chapter for overseeing the burial, which must be presented by Daoist priests who have received the *破殓箓* (Pòliàn Luó—burial and exorcism talismans) to the Celestial Bureau.

Since this contains sacred scriptures and rites, it must not be shown to outsiders, and should be burned within one or two days.  If the deceased was not a great sage or renowned figure, there will inevitably be no descendants to perform the three *三礼* (Sān Lǐ—three ritual observances: mourning, sacrifice, and memorial) or the three *三契* (Sān Qì—three solemn oaths: to uphold filial piety, to maintain ancestral rites, and to honor the deceased).  These rites are not easily accomplished by all.  This memorial chapter must be clearly understood; any deviation from proper procedure leaves one powerless and without recourse.  I therefore compose it in accordance with the prescribed *三归* (Sān Guī—Three Refuges), briefly setting forth the essential points.

Once the deceased has been placed in the coffin, first follow the *移书* (Yíshū—burial transfer document) as written by Master Meng, then spread a mat at the coffin’s head and light incense, striking the small *磬子* (qìngzi—stone chime) three times.

After the coffin has been sealed, kneel at length and recount the circumstances of the deceased’s life.  I hear that the Dao is fundamentally without birth, yet born it must die—like lightning in the clouds or waves upon the sea, fleeting and not bound by human expectation, its depths unknown to mortal understanding.  In their brief time together, the living leaned upon the deceased as kin, but now each moment passes, leaving none as steadfast guardians.  All depart, leaving the living in sorrow.

Today, the deceased—my fateful companion—was granted birth in this holy age, heard the scriptures, and beheld the Dao.  With sincere devotion, I cultivated the True Way, bowing daily to its *龙章* (Long Zhāng—Dragon Script) and morning and evening to its *凤篆* (Fèng Zhuàn—Phoenix Seal), hoping to ascend like the crane and dwell eternally among the clouds.  Who could have foreseen that the *孔水* (Kǒng Shuǐ—Great Water) would shorten their years, and the *庄山* (Zhuāng Shān—Mourning Mountain) would hasten their end?  In an instant, they abandoned this life, and now forever stray from the path of rebirth.

In grief, I rise and fall without direction, weeping before their garments and coffin.  Once the burial attire is properly arranged, I entrust them to the coffin with the *良时* (Liáng Shí—auspicious hour).

I now list all the required burial documents in strict accordance with the *丧礼* (Sàng Lǐ—funeral rites).  The *移文* (Yíwén—burial transfer document) is read.  After reading the *移文*, the Three Refuges are intoned.

For the deceased, I take refuge in the Tenfold Great Dao, praying that their divine presence may descend, uprooting the sevenfold karmic roots of suffering and freeing them from the bonds of the Three Realms.  May they wander in wondrous fields, recognizing the celestial officials, and prostrate themselves before the peerless, boundless Dao.  I take refuge in the Thirty-Six Sacred Scriptures, praying that the luminous texts may nourish their spirit and refine their nature, that the Dragon Script and Phoenix Seal may illuminate their inner chambers, and that they may forever depart from the Northern Darkness and dwell in the Southern Extremity.  I prostrate myself before the宗玄自然法宝 (Zōng Xuán Zìrán Fǎbǎo—Supreme Mysterious Natural Dharma Treasure).

I take refuge in the later sages and all accomplished Daoist masters, that their sevenfold enlightenment may radiate brightly and their sixfold transcendent wisdom illuminate all.  May they universally save both the hidden and the manifest, guiding all through life and death.  I prostrate myself before the Tenfold Boundless Saints.

When the rites are complete, place the *移文* at the coffin’s head, and leave the rest to the master of the household to secure the coffin with nails and cover it with the two *孟* (Mèng—burial mats).  If, on the day of burial, I arrive late, the coffin must be moved to the hall, where a white cloth curtain is set before it.

After three days of mourning, once the burial garments are complete, arrange the spirit tablet (*灵*—Líng) to the south of the coffin, facing east, with a screen, couch, and burial attire all prepared as in life.  Offer meals at dawn and midday.

Before the burial garments are complete, the filial children must, at the spirit tablet’s southern end, gather the deceased’s hair with a cloth, then place the tablet facing north, with east as the superior direction.  Day and night, they must weep, and upon completion of the burial garments and removal of the tablet, bow and prostrate themselves again.  After bowing, proceed to the coffin and weep.  Having exhausted their grief, return to the tablet’s southern end to lean and weep; if their sorrow is light, face west at the tablet and lean to weep.  With north as the superior direction, perform the rites of condolence and consolation.

If these rites are followed strictly, they will be difficult to comprehend.  Not only will the filial children be confused by the northward and westward directions, but those offering condolences will also harbor doubts.

Now, I adjust these rites: before the burial garments are complete, gather the hair with the cloth and place the tablet to the north of the coffin, setting a white cloth curtain before it, with men to the left and women to the right.  After three days, once the burial garments are complete, arrange the spirit tablet to the south of the coffin, with a screen and couch.  This way, not only will those offering condolences see it, but the filial children on either side will also find it appropriate.

The rites of Master Zhang are similar to these.  This concludes the sixth chapter on completing the burial garments.»

Upon receiving the Daoist precepts, one must complete the *jue* (箓). For the Three Five (三五) and Yuanming (元命) initiations, there are eight *quan* (券) and ten *jue* (箓). The eight *quan* are: the *Dingzhi Quan* (定志券), *Shengxuan Quan* (升玄券), *Maizi Quan* (卖子券), *Qieri Quan* (乞儿券), and the four *Shangqing Quan* (上清四券): *Xueye Quan* (血液券), *Shenxian Quan* (神仙券), *Dadu Quan* (大度券), and *Tongling Quan* (通灵券). The ten *jue* include: *Chuman Jue* (除满箓), *Sanwu Jue* (三五箓), *Yuanming Jue* (元命箓), as well as *Zhanxie Jue* (斩邪箓), *Pozhong Bai Gui Zhao Jue* (破殗百鬼召箓), *Duzhang Bi Yin Jue* (都章毕印箓), *Jiuzhou She Ling Jue* (九州社令箓), *Tai Xuan Si Bu Xian* (太玄四部线), *Hetu Jue* (河图箓), *Qingjia Jue* (青甲箓), *Dongye Da Jin Jue* (东野大禁箓), and *Shi Jiangjun Jue* (十将军箓).

If following the rites of the Small Meng (小孟), the above are observed. For the Great Meng (大孟), there are neither the eight *quan* nor the ten *jue*—only the ten *jue* as per Master Shiji (石井公), without the eight *quan*. Following the Small Meng, the mourning garments are the same as those of the Three Months (丝麻三月服), with men and women using fifteen *sheng* (升) of cloth for their robes and headgear. However, the Great Meng and Master Shiji both state: "The teaching of *Famen* (发蒙) is deeply gracious and heavy with favor. The rites of mourning and receiving condolences are established without the completion of mourning garments." Today, I adhere to the *Yijing* (《易》), which says: "A child comes seeking me; it is not I who seek the child. If I do not teach the child, it will eventually face ruin." The *Shengxuan Jing* (《升玄经》) states: "Both the former teacher and the latter teacher must be respected. How can they be neglected? This is because they share the same precepts yet are suddenly severed." Following the Small Meng, three months are deemed sufficient for this respect.

The *Wuqian Wen* (五千文), *Dajie Bai Bashi Lu* (大诫百八十律), *Zhenwei* (真诰), *Wuyue* (五岳), *Liujia* (六甲), and *Jinshan* (禁山) rites are uniformly prescribed by both Small and Great Meng. For the Small Funeral (小功), five months of mourning are required. Men and women use eleven *sheng* of cloth for their lower garments and twelve *sheng* for their headgear. Today, people merely wear headbands instead of headgear, simply to save effort.

According to Confucian rites, mourning garments transform into auspicious ones. Auspicious headgear is worn on the head, the *gu* (着) upper garment is worn on the body, and the lower garment is worn beneath. If following the Small and Great Meng, twelve *sheng* of cloth are used for the headgear, while eleven *sheng* are used for the *he* (褐) or *qun* (裙). However, Master Shiji states: "These mourning garments are the same for the Small Funeral, five months in duration, with men wearing silk headbands and women wearing linen lower garments. If people now wear headbands, why not use silk?" Those who claim to follow the Daoist teachings yet wear coarse garments of cloth, and upon their teacher's death, don linen lower garments, not only violate the principles of the Way but also defy common human sentiment. If it is indeed the Small Funeral, five months of mourning, why then adopt silk as a garment? The *Sangfu Yi* (《丧服仪》) states: "The *Zimá* (缌麻) garments are woven with fine threads like silk, but are actually made of hemp, hence the term *Zimá* (腮麻); the Small Funeral garments use small threads, but with greater effort, hence the term *Small Funeral* (小功); the Great Funeral garments use large threads, with lesser effort, hence the term *Great Funeral* (大功)." Though Master Shiji has this stipulation, I do not adopt it.

I request adherence to the Small and Great Meng rites: before burial, wear cloth headgear and cloth upper garments; after burial, wear cloth headbands and cloth blankets. The *Shenbao Da Meng* (灵宝大盟), *Zhenjing San Pin* (真经三品), and other scriptures such as *Ziran* (自然), *Zhongmei* (中盟), *Sanhuang* (三皇), *Wufu* (五符), *Qichuan* (七传), and *Baoshén* (宝神) are uniformly prescribed. The *Zhenjing* (真经) refers to the *Shangqing Shoudao Jing* (上情大洞真经), which contains two sets of precepts, totaling thirty-five volumes. The three *jue* are: 1) *Basu Quci Jue* (八素曲辞箓), 2) *Shanghuang Yuzhu Jue* (上皇玉箓), and 3) the *Sanjian Zhengfa Jue* (三天正法箓); the three *ben* (奔) are: Sun *ben* (日奔), Moon *ben* (月奔), and Star *ben* (星奔), which are also called the three *jing* (景). Upon receiving the Three Five precepts, one ascends to the *Shangyuqing Jing* (上玉清景), *Shangqing Jing* (上清景), and *Taiping Jing* (太清景). Today, upon receiving the three *ben*, one may be called a disciple of the three *jing*. These rites are uniformly prescribed by both Small and Great Meng.

For the mourning period of *Qisui* (齐衰), men and women use four *sheng* of cloth for their robes and seven *sheng* for their headgear, with a three-year period of inner mourning, abstaining from music and joyous gatherings. If one has previously received the *Wuqian Wen* (五千文), *Ziran Da Meng* (自然大盟), and other scriptural precepts, and all were received from the same teacher, then they should all observe the *Zhanxui* (斩衰) mourning for three years, adhering to the most solemn rites. In eating, drinking, speaking, listening, resting, and dwelling, they should all follow the original rites of filial children, completing the mourning period within two years, then donning auspicious garments without any remaining mourning rites.

Today, those who receive the *Da Meng* (大盟) precepts often merely let their hair down for one period. Those who receive the *Shangqing* (上清) precepts either disregard the mourning rites entirely or observe only three days of mourning, or claim nine months of mourning. Not only do they fail to recognize the three *pin* (品) and three *ben* (奔) and three *jue* (箓), but they also do not know which scriptures the three *pin* refer to. They merely follow the *Qichuan* (七传), which is a branch of the *Shangqing* teachings, and thus observe nine months of mourning. Yet, they do not even see the scriptures, let alone comprehend them thoroughly. Their teachers, seeking wide renown, teach lightly, while the disciples, valuing purple robes, compete to receive precepts.

Master Tao (陶先生) said: "Ancient people valued the skin of the scriptures; I command them to value the ears' learning." Truly, these words are profound. How can one who receives the *Shangqing* precepts, yet fails to recognize the scriptures upon seeing them, and not knowing the rites, dare to slight their teacher?

The *Shangqing* and *Damen* are both true scriptures, both retaining heavy mourning rites. Let each reflect carefully upon the two *Meng* (孟) for long deliberation.

**Preceptors and Scribes**
The two *Meng* state: When two persons discuss mourning rites, the *jing* preceptor (经师) performs minor rites, while the scribe (籍师) performs major rites—this also depends on the weight of the teacher.
To determine the weight of the teacher:
- Serving as a teacher for three years equals one period as a scribe.
- Serving as a teacher for one period equals major rites as a scribe.
- Serving as a teacher for major rites equals one period as a *jing* preceptor.
- Serving as a teacher for minor rites equals major rites as a *jing* preceptor.
- Serving as a teacher for three days equals minor rites as a *jing* preceptor.
- Serving as a teacher for three months equals no rites as a *jing* preceptor.
If a teacher serves as a scribe, all such disciples are treated lightly, and the teacher is also treated lightly—each case is reduced accordingly.

**Shared Mourning Rites**
The above rites follow the two *Meng*, with three months of mourning for *zaimá* (齐衰) rites.

**Teacher as Disciple**
The two *Meng* state: If a teacher becomes a disciple, the example is one rank lower—three years of service equals one period of mourning.
For *zaimá* (齐衰), nine months; for major rites (*dàgōng*, 大功), five months; for minor rites (*xiǎogōng*, 小功), three months.
Master Shíjǐng (石井公) said: "Mourning rites for repaying a teacher are the same as for a disciple, except that the hair is not shaved."

As for common rites, which emphasize humility toward superiors, the two *Meng* are correct. If a teacher is elderly and cannot endure mourning, heart-mourning alone suffices.
The two *Meng* state: "Within our sect, disciples mourn their teachers according to scriptural teachings, for the teacher has liberated them from birth-and-death, and their debt exceeds that to parents. Thus, their mourning differs from common rites. Since there is no suffering, it does not violate the scriptures. All other mourning rites follow the law’s weight and are established as institutions."
Master Shíjǐng said: "The nourishment of repaying mourning rites—whether for minor or major rites—requires shaving the hair, regardless of whether one has received the precepts. If one is already in mourning, they should shave their hair at the appropriate time."

**Seventh Rite: Funeral Procession**
If the deceased is a great sage or renowned person, follow the *Zhènjīng* (镇经) and *Wǔliàn Shēngshī Jīng* (五练生尸经), pre-writing five *shí* (石) of offerings, and proceed according to the scriptures—this is not detailed here.

On the burial day, perform the funeral rites with the *Rùzhǒng Jīngbǎo* (入冢经宝), then arrange offerings. Incense, flowers, banners, musicians, and chanting are arranged in order before the procession, with the Daoist disciples following the coffin.

Next, the soul tablet is carried, with banners and flowers in front, followed by the third offering of food, then the funeral carriage.
After the procession, the most respected filial son (who is also a disciple) leads, followed by lesser disciples. If there are commoners, the most filial son is last. If traveling by boat, the most respected should be at the rear.

Ensure the order is auspicious in front and inauspicious behind, proceeding step-by-step according to the time.

**Burial and Placement Rites**
Place the coffin in the center of the grave, install the scriptures at the front of the tomb, arrange offerings according to the law, and set up the soul couch within the tomb. Place the sacrificial altar in front, with male filial sons to the left and female filial sons to the right. Follow the seven *zhèng* (正) of Dao, facing the tomb, and recite scriptures, repent, and offer incense.

Once all arrangements are complete, after the vegetarian meal is finished, the disciples recite the funeral scripture before the altar, complete the mourning, and then withdraw the scriptures into the tomb. If the distance is far, adjust accordingly.

After placing the coffin, install a stone couch at the head of the coffin, place a table on it, and store the scriptures inside. Place a child immortal (*xiān tóng*, 仙童) on the left, a jade maiden (*yù nǚ*, 玉女) on the right, and divine soldiers and horses at the tomb’s entrance. Install a square plaque, along with a small table, paper, brush, ink, handkerchief, incense, and candles. The filial sons all bow, deliver the farewell words, seal the tomb, and then perform the condolence rites before returning.

All the above are the rites established by the two *Meng*.
Common funeral customs vary with wealth and poverty, but when examining true scriptures, there is no fixed rule. Indeed.

*"Huanhún* water is placed above, *ān fǎnpò* fire below.
Anciently, the wild was used as a coffin; Zhuang Zhou took the heavens and earth as a coffin—this is not merely about ritual laws and numbers, but about what the common people see and hear.

If one values virtue and morality in life, they will naturally disregard the body in death. Adapt to circumstances, increase or decrease as needed—it is not bound by the numbers one, one, three, or four."

**Eighth Rite: Soul Couch Arrangement**
The two *Meng* state: Arrange the soul couch’s position as follows:
A five-*chǐ* couch faces south, with screens, curtains, and drapes. Clothing and belongings follow the seasonal laws.
Place an orange wood box (*chéng*, 橙) before the couch, arranging incense, handkerchiefs, seals, combs, brushes, and garments.
In the morning, place a small table and mat; in the evening, move to another couch and bed, and arrange pillows and quilts.
In the morning, roll them up again. For meals, offer betel leaves, pure water, and handkerchiefs—just as when the deceased was alive.
All seasonal items are displayed as needed.

The two *Meng* state: If a prince, king, father, uncle, or elder teacher comes to condole, the filial son should welcome them outside the door and guide them back through a separate path.
The mourner leans and weeps at the eastern head of the couch, with the north considered superior.
Next, lesser filial sons lean and weep before the couch, facing north, with the east considered superior.
The honored guest is seated in the guest position, with each seat’s east side considered superior. When the five-tone wailing arrives, the filial son kneels to receive them.
After the guest pats their hand and bows twice, they are escorted back to the welcoming point and resume weeping.
If the guest is a commoner of high status, no bowing is required.
If the guest is of moderate status, they simply go to the eastern head of the couch to weep, and after the guest finishes wailing, the filial son kneels to receive them by the hand. After the guest departs, the filial son weeps again.
If the guest is of average status, they sit in order and weep, receiving the guest’s condolences.
For low-status guests, they only bow and do not hold hands.
For moderately respectful guests, they kneel and hold hands.
For guests of equal status, they lean and hold hands.
If the guest has a familial connection within the Daoist or secular world, after condolences, they must also lean and weep before the couch before departing.
If the guest has not seen the filial son since the death and has not yet paid their respects, even if they are of equal status, they must go outside to lean and weep to receive the condolences.

**Ninth Rite: Disciples Returning to Their Original Observances**

**Second Rule on the Second Month:**
If the mourning period falls outside the proper season and disciples return to their original observatories, then place a lower couch at the northern wall, facing south, with the west side as the upper end, and sit in mourning to receive condolences.

If the guest is of high status, kneel to receive and hold hands; if of equal status, sit to receive condolences, arranging the guest’s seat at the lower western wall, placing a five-foot couch facing east with its back open. The guest then faces east and ascends the couch in order, with the northern side as the upper end. They prostrate themselves and weep five times before rising to offer condolences, and the filial son kneels to hold hands as before.

In modern practice, since no floating spirit tablet (*fúling*) is installed, the condolences are received in the empty hall. The filial son resides in the bedroom, placing a plain lower couch, removing the old screens and curtains, and encircling the couch with reeds and horsehair. The door is hung with white cloth curtains, and the mourner speaks softly without engaging in conversation.

**Twelfth Rule on Removing Mourning Attire:**
From the first seventh-day, second seventh-day, third seventh-day, up to the hundredth day and first anniversary, according to one’s merit and virtue, once these periods have passed, no further offerings are made in the morning or midday—only incense is kept burning until the second anniversary, at which point mourning attire may be removed.

If one says, *"I am satisfied and may remove mourning attire,"* know that there is a deeper meaning to not removing it. Without renown or virtue, why would one linger on the couch so long?

Master Zhang says: Install an incense burner, and let the disciples weep and wail morning and evening. Within three days, prepare a vegetarian meal, and offer it at midday, continuing until the fourth day. Then place a basin of clear water. Upon reaching the hundredth day, dismantle the couch and remove mourning attire according to the severity of the loss.

If the deceased was of notable virtue, even at the hundredth day, fear that the grief may not yet be fully expressed.

If one attains the highest virtue today, revered by all under heaven, even though the body may be laid aside like discarded sandals and the spirit ascends to the celestial gates, all will mourn and honor them, gazing upon the memorial couch with awe.

If one chooses to remain on the couch for an extended period, deviating from customary practice, then among disciples who have received the Dao to its utmost and wear the *jiancui* mourning robe—these may remain on the couch for a second year; if disciples have completed only one mourning period, they may remain on the couch for one year; if they mourn for nine months, they may remain on the couch for nine months; if they mourn for five months, they may remain on the couch for five months; if some mourn for three months, they may remain on the couch for a hundred days.

If the deceased was poor and had no descendants, even if the couch and memorial table are set up, no offerings are made, and dust and filth accumulate. Though the couch may be easily arranged, removing it will be difficult. If grief proves unbearable, one may dissolve the mourning according to the circumstances.

If the deceased was of notable virtue, yet not surpassing others, and had no descendants, the funeral rites may be conducted as is proper, and the mourning may follow customary practice—there is no need for an extended memorial couch.

If the deceased was utterly detached from worldly affairs, their reputation fading like a bubble and their form as fleeting as a dream, seeking no grand burial and requiring no memorial table, then if there are no posthumous instructions, each may mourn according to their own capacity.

Thus concludes the sixteenth chapter of the *Miaojuan* on the subtle precepts of rites, laws, and disciplines.
